<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg"><img alt="A photograph of four members of The Red Hot Chili Peppers performing on a stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="640" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/250px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/375px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/500px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Red_Hot_Chili_Peppers" title="Red Hot Chili Peppers">Red Hot Chili Peppers</a> in 2006, showing a quartet lineup for a rock band (from left to right: bassist, lead vocalist, drummer, and guitarist).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The sound of rock is traditionally centered on the <a href="/wiki/Guitar_amplifier" title="Guitar amplifier">amplified</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitar</a>, which emerged in its modern form in the 1950s with the popularization of rock and roll,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and was influenced by the sounds of <a href="/wiki/Electric_blues" title="Electric blues">electric blues</a> guitarists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-5">[5]</a></sup> The sound of an electric guitar in rock music is typically supported by an electric <a href="/wiki/Bass_guitar" title="Bass guitar">bass guitar</a>, which pioneered in jazz music in the same era,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and percussion produced from a <a href="/wiki/Drum_kit" title="Drum kit">drum kit</a> that combines drums and cymbals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> This trio of instruments has often been complemented by the inclusion of other instruments, particularly keyboards such as the <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammond_organ" title="Hammond organ">Hammond organ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The basic rock instrumentation was adapted from the basic <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> band instrumentation (prominent <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">lead guitar</a>, second chordal instrument, bass, and drums).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-5">[5]</a></sup> A group of musicians performing rock music is termed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_band" title="Rock band">rock band</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_group" title="Rock group">rock group</a> and typically consists of between three–the <a href="/wiki/Power_trio" title="Power trio">power trio</a> used in rock, metal and punk rock–and five members. Classically, a rock band takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Quartet" title="Quartet">quartet</a> whose members cover one or more roles, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocals" title="Vocals">vocalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">lead guitarist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_guitar" title="Rhythm guitar">rhythm guitarist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bass_guitarist" title="Bass guitarist">bass guitarist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drummer" title="Drummer">drummer</a> and often that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keyboard_player" title="Keyboard player">keyboard player</a> or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instrumentalist" title="Instrumentalist">instrumentalist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rock music is traditionally built on a foundation of simple unsyncopated rhythms in a 4/4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter_(music)" title="Meter (music)">meter</a>, with a repetitive snare drum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Backbeat_(music)" title="Backbeat (music)">back beat</a> on beats two and four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-10">[10]</a></sup> Melodies are often derived from older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_modes" title="Musical modes">musical modes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_mode" title="Dorian mode">Dorian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixolydian" title="Mixolydian">Mixolydian</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_mode" title="Minor mode">minor</a> modes. Harmonies range from the common <a href="/wiki/Triad_(music)" title="Triad (music)">triad</a> to parallel <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">fourths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">fifths</a> and dissonant harmonic progressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-10">[10]</a></sup> Rock songs, since the late 1950s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and particularly from the mid-1960s onwards, often used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verse-chorus_form" title="Verse-chorus form">verse-chorus structure</a> derived from blues and folk music, but there has been considerable variation from this model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Critics have stressed the eclecticism and stylistic diversity of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Because of its complex history and tendency to borrow from other musical and cultural forms, it has been argued that "it is impossible to bind rock music to a rigidly delineated musical definition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" data-file-width="845" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png/220px-Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png/330px-Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png/440px-Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A simple 4/4 drum pattern common in rock music <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_duple_drum_pattern.mid" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Simple_duple_drum_pattern.mid" title="Simple duple drum pattern.mid">Play</a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Simple_duple_drum_pattern.mid" title="File:Simple duple drum pattern.mid"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike many earlier styles of popular music, rock lyrics have dealt with a wide range of themes in addition to romantic love: including sex, rebellion against "<a href="/wiki/The_Establishment" title="The Establishment">The Establishment</a>", social concerns and life styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-10">[10]</a></sup> These themes were inherited from a variety of sources, including the <a href="/wiki/Tin_Pan_Alley" title="Tin Pan Alley">Tin Pan Alley</a> pop tradition, folk music and rhythm and blues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Music journalist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> characterizes rock lyrics as a "cool medium" with simple diction and repeated refrains, and asserts that rock's primary "function" "pertains to music, or, more generally, <a href="/wiki/Noise_in_music" title="Noise in music">noise</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The predominance of white, male and often middle class musicians in rock music has often been noted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and rock has been seen as an appropriation of black musical forms for a young, white and largely male audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> As a result, it has been seen as articulating the concerns of this group in both style and lyrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the term rock began to be used in preference to rock and roll from the late-1960s, it has often been contrasted with pop music, with which it has shared many characteristics, but from which it is often distanced by an emphasis on musicianship, live performance and a focus on serious and progressive themes as part of an ideology of authenticity that is frequently combined with an awareness of the genre's history and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-20">[20]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Simon_Frith" title="Simon Frith">Simon Frith</a> "rock was something more than pop, something more than rock and roll. Rock musicians combined an emphasis on skill and technique with the romantic concept of art as artistic expression, original and sincere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-20">[20]</a></sup> In the new millennium the term <i>rock</i> has sometimes been used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanket_term" title="Blanket term">blanket term</a> including forms such as <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reggae_music" title="Reggae music">reggae music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a>, with which it has been influenced but often contrasted through much of its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock_and_roll">Rock and roll</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rock and roll">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">Rock and roll</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_rock_and_roll" title="Origins of rock and roll">Origins of rock and roll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rockabilly" title="Rockabilly">Rockabilly</a></div>
<p>The foundations of rock music are in rock and roll, which originated in the United States during the late 1940s and early 1950s, and quickly spread to much of the rest of the world. Its immediate origins lay in a melding of various <a href="/wiki/African-American_music" title="African-American music">black musical</a> genres of the time, including <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel music</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country and western</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BrithofR.26R_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BrithofR.26R-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1951, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland, Ohio</a> disc jockey <a href="/wiki/Alan_Freed" title="Alan Freed">Alan Freed</a> began playing rhythm and blues music (then termed "race music") for a multi-racial audience, and is credited with first using the phrase "rock and roll" to describe the music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Debate surrounds which record should be considered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_rock_and_roll_record" title="First rock and roll record">first rock and roll record</a>. Contenders include <a href="/wiki/Goree_Carter" title="Goree Carter">Goree Carter</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/File:Goree_Carter_-_Rock_Awhile.ogg" title="File:Goree Carter - Rock Awhile.ogg">Rock Awhile</a>" (1949);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer19_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer19-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Preston" title="Jimmy Preston">Jimmy Preston</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Rock_the_Joint" title="Rock the Joint">Rock the Joint</a>" (1949), which was later <a href="/wiki/Cover_version" title="Cover version">covered</a> by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Haley_%26_His_Comets" title="Bill Haley &amp; His Comets">Bill Haley &amp; His Comets</a> in 1952;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Rocket_88" title="Rocket 88">Rocket 88</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Brenston" title="Jackie Brenston">Jackie Brenston</a> and his Delta Cats (in fact, <a href="/wiki/Ike_Turner" title="Ike Turner">Ike Turner</a> and his band the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Rhythm" title="Kings of Rhythm">Kings of Rhythm</a>), recorded by <a href="/wiki/Sam_Phillips" title="Sam Phillips">Sam Phillips</a> for <a href="/wiki/Sun_Records" title="Sun Records">Sun Records</a> in 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2008_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2008-26">[26]</a></sup> Four years later, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Haley" title="Bill Haley">Bill Haley</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Rock_Around_the_Clock" title="Rock Around the Clock">Rock Around the Clock</a>" (1955) became the first rock and roll song to top <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> magazine's main sales and airplay charts, and opened the door worldwide for this new wave of popular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55.2C_track_2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55.2C_track_2-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Elvis Presley standing between two sets of bars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3448" data-file-width="2673" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/170px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/255px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/340px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> in a promotion shot for <i><a href="/wiki/Jailhouse_Rock_(film)" title="Jailhouse Rock (film)">Jailhouse Rock</a></i> in 1957</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It has also been argued that "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/That%27s_All_Right_(Mama)" title="That's All Right (Mama)">That's All Right (Mama)</a>" (1954), <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>'s first single for Sun Records in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, could be the first rock and roll record,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> but, at the same time, <a href="/wiki/Big_Joe_Turner" title="Big Joe Turner">Big Joe Turner</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shake,_Rattle_%26_Roll" title="Shake, Rattle &amp; Roll">Shake, Rattle &amp; Roll</a>", later covered by Haley, was already at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Hot_R%26B/Hip-Hop_Songs" title="Hot R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Songs">Billboard R&amp;B charts</a>. Other artists with early rock and roll hits included <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bo_Diddley" title="Bo Diddley">Bo Diddley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fats_Domino" title="Fats Domino">Fats Domino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Lee_Lewis" title="Jerry Lee Lewis">Jerry Lee Lewis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Vincent" title="Gene Vincent">Gene Vincent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2008_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2008-26">[26]</a></sup> Soon rock and roll was the major force in American record sales and <a href="/wiki/Crooner" title="Crooner">crooners</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Fisher_(singer)" title="Eddie Fisher (singer)">Eddie Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perry_Como" title="Perry Como">Perry Como</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patti_Page" title="Patti Page">Patti Page</a>, who had dominated the previous decade of popular music, found their access to the pop charts significantly curtailed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rock and roll has been seen as leading to a number of distinct subgenres, including rockabilly, combining rock and roll with "hillbilly" country music, which was usually played and recorded in the mid-1950s by white singers such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Perkins" title="Carl Perkins">Carl Perkins</a>, Jerry Lee Lewis, <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Holly" title="Buddy Holly">Buddy Holly</a> and with the greatest commercial success, Elvis Presley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicRbilly_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicRbilly-31">[31]</a></sup> In contrast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_wop" title="Doo wop">doo wop</a> placed an emphasis on multi-part vocal harmonies and meaningless backing lyrics (from which the genre later gained its name), which were usually supported with light instrumentation and had its origins in 1930s and 1940s African American vocal groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Shuker.2C_2005_p._35_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-R._Shuker.2C_2005_p._35-32">[32]</a></sup> Acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Crows" title="The Crows">the Crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Penguins" title="The Penguins">the Penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_El_Dorados" title="The El Dorados">the El Dorados</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Turbans" title="The Turbans">the Turbans</a> all scored major hits, and groups like <a href="/wiki/The_Platters" title="The Platters">the Platters</a>, with songs including "<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Pretender" title="The Great Pretender">The Great Pretender</a>" (1955),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_5.2C_track_3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_5.2C_track_3-33">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Coasters" title="The Coasters">the Coasters</a> with humorous songs like "<a href="/wiki/Yakety_Yak" title="Yakety Yak">Yakety Yak</a>" (1958),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_13_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_13-34">[34]</a></sup> ranked among the most successful rock and roll acts of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002DooWop_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002DooWop-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The era also saw the growth in popularity of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitar</a>, and the development of a specifically rock and roll style of playing through such exponents as Chuck Berry, <a href="/wiki/Link_Wray" title="Link Wray">Link Wray</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scotty_Moore" title="Scotty Moore">Scotty Moore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">distortion</a>, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Electric_blues" title="Electric blues">electric blues</a> guitarists such as <a href="/wiki/Guitar_Slim" title="Guitar Slim">Guitar Slim</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aswellslim_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-aswellslim-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Johnson_(guitarist)" title="Willie Johnson (guitarist)">Willie Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Hare" title="Pat Hare">Pat Hare</a> in the early 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-38">[38]</a></sup> was popularized by Chuck Berry in the mid-1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">power chords</a>, pioneered by Willie Johnson and Pat Hare in the early 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-38">[38]</a></sup> was popularized by Link Wray in the late 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Trad_jazz" title="Trad jazz">trad jazz</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_folk_music" title="English folk music">folk</a> movements brought visiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blues_music" title="Blues music">blues music</a> artists to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz2007_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz2007-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lonnie_Donegan" title="Lonnie Donegan">Lonnie Donegan</a>'s 1955 hit "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_Island_Line_(song)" title="Rock Island Line (song)">Rock Island Line</a>" was a major influence and helped to develop the trend of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skiffle_music" title="Skiffle music">skiffle music</a> groups throughout the country, many of which, including <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Quarrymen" title="The Quarrymen">Quarrymen</a>, moved on to play rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commentators have traditionally perceived a decline of rock and roll in the late 1950s and early 1960s. By 1959, the death of Buddy Holly, <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Bopper" title="The Big Bopper">The Big Bopper</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richie_Valens" title="Richie Valens">Richie Valens</a> in a plane crash, the departure of Elvis for the army, the retirement of Little Richard to become a preacher, prosecutions of Jerry Lee Lewis and Chuck Berry and the breaking of the <a href="/wiki/Payola" title="Payola">payola</a> scandal (which implicated major figures, including Alan Freed, in bribery and corruption in promoting individual acts or songs), gave a sense that the rock and roll era established at that point had come to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2008p99_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2008p99-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22In-between_years.22">"In-between years"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: "In-between years"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">Rock and roll</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_Wop" title="Doo Wop">Doo Wop</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_rock_and_roll" title="British rock and roll">British rock and roll</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">Soul music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Shirelles_1962.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="780" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/The_Shirelles_1962.jpg/140px-The_Shirelles_1962.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/The_Shirelles_1962.jpg/210px-The_Shirelles_1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/The_Shirelles_1962.jpg/280px-The_Shirelles_1962.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Shirelles_1962.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Shirelles" title="The Shirelles">The Shirelles</a> in 1962. Clockwise from top: Addie "Micki" Harris, <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Owens" title="Shirley Owens">Shirley Owens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Lee" title="Beverly Lee">Beverly Lee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doris_Coley" title="Doris Coley">Doris Coley</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The period of the later 1950s and early 1960s, between the end of the initial period of innovation and what became known in the US as the "<a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a>", has traditionally been seen as an era of hiatus for rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_20-21_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_20-21-44">[44]</a></sup> More recently some authors have emphasised important innovations and trends in this period without which future developments would not have been possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-46">[46]</a></sup> While early rock and roll, particularly through the advent of rockabilly, saw the greatest commercial success for male and white performers, in this era the genre was dominated by black and female artists. Rock and roll had not disappeared at the end of the 1950s and some of its energy can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Twist_(dance)" title="Twist (dance)">Twist</a> dance craze of the early 1960s, mainly benefiting the career of <a href="/wiki/Chubby_Checker" title="Chubby Checker">Chubby Checker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-46">[46]</a></sup> Having died down in the late 1950s, doo wop enjoyed a revival in the same period, with hits for acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Marcels" title="The Marcels">the Marcels</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Capris" title="The Capris">the Capris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Williams_and_the_Zodiacs" title="Maurice Williams and the Zodiacs">Maurice Williams and the Zodiacs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shep_and_the_Limelights" title="Shep and the Limelights">Shep and the Limelights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002DooWop_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002DooWop-35">[35]</a></sup> The rise of <a href="/wiki/Girl_group" title="Girl group">girl groups</a> like <a href="/wiki/The_Chantels" title="The Chantels">the Chantels</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Shirelles" title="The Shirelles">the Shirelles</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Crystals" title="The Crystals">the Crystals</a> placed an emphasis on harmonies and polished production that was in contrast to earlier rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Some of the most significant girl group hits were products of the <a href="/wiki/Brill_Building" title="Brill Building">Brill Building</a> Sound, named after the block in New York where many songwriters were based, which included the number 1 hit for the Shirelles "<a href="/wiki/Will_You_Love_Me_Tomorrow" title="Will You Love Me Tomorrow">Will You Love Me Tomorrow</a>" in 1960, penned by the partnership of <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Goffin" title="Gerry Goffin">Gerry Goffin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Brill_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Brill-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cliff_Richard" title="Cliff Richard">Cliff Richard</a> had the first <a href="/wiki/British_rock_and_roll" title="British rock and roll">British rock and roll</a> hit with "<a href="/wiki/Move_It" title="Move It">Move It</a>", effectively ushering in the sound of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_rock" title="British rock">British rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> At the start of the 1960s, his backing group <a href="/wiki/The_Shadows" title="The Shadows">the Shadows</a> was the most successful group recording instrumentals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> While rock 'n' roll was fading into lightweight pop and ballads, British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_group" title="Rock group">rock groups</a> at clubs and local dances, heavily influenced by blues-rock pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Korner" title="Alexis Korner">Alexis Korner</a>, were starting to play with an intensity and drive seldom found in white American acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also significant was the advent of <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a> as a major commercial force. Developing out of rhythm and blues with a re-injection of gospel music and pop, led by pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Ray_Charles" title="Ray Charles">Ray Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Cooke" title="Sam Cooke">Sam Cooke</a> from the mid-1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55.2C_track_3.3B_shows_15-17_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55.2C_track_3.3B_shows_15-17-52">[52]</a></sup> by the early 1960s figures like <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Gaye" title="Marvin Gaye">Marvin Gaye</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">Aretha Franklin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curtis_Mayfield" title="Curtis Mayfield">Curtis Mayfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a> were dominating the R&amp;B charts and breaking through into the main pop charts, helping to accelerate their desegregation, while <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stax/Volt" title="Stax/Volt">Stax/Volt</a> Records were becoming major forces in the record industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Soul_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Soul-53">[53]</a></sup> All of these elements, including the close harmonies of doo wop and girl groups, the carefully crafted song-writing of the Brill Building Sound and the polished production values of soul, have been seen as influencing the <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">Merseybeat</a> sound, particularly the early work of <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, and through them the form of later rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Mersey_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Mersey-54">[54]</a></sup> Some historians of music have also pointed to important and innovative technical developments that built on rock and roll in this period, including the electronic treatment of sound by such innovators as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Meek" title="Joe Meek">Joe Meek</a>, and the elaborate production methods of the <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> pursued by <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">Phil Spector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_1960s">Early 1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surf_music">Surf music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Surf music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Surf_music" title="Surf music">Surf music</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section should include only a <strong>brief</strong> summary of <a href="/wiki/Surf_music" title="Surf music">Surf music</a>.</b> See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">Wikipedia:Summary style</a> for information on how to properly incorporate it into this article's main text. <small><i>(August 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The instrumental rock and roll of performers such as <a href="/wiki/Duane_Eddy" title="Duane Eddy">Duane Eddy</a>, Link Wray and <a href="/wiki/The_Ventures" title="The Ventures">the Ventures</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Dick_Dale" title="Dick Dale">Dick Dale</a>, who added distinctive "wet" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverb" title="Reverb">reverb</a>, rapid alternate picking, and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_music" title="Middle Eastern music">Middle Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Mexico" title="Music of Mexico">Mexican</a> influences. He produced the regional hit "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go_Trippin%27" title="Let's Go Trippin'">Let's Go Trippin'</a>" in 1961 and launched the surf music craze, following up with songs like "<a href="/wiki/Misirlou" title="Misirlou">Misirlou</a>" (1962).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-blair2-56">[56]</a></sup> Like Dale and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Del-Tones" title="Del-Tones">Del-Tones</a>, most early surf bands were formed in Southern California, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bel-Airs" title="Bel-Airs">Bel-Airs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/The_Challengers_(band)" title="The Challengers (band)">Challengers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_%26_the_Showmen" title="Eddie &amp; the Showmen">Eddie &amp; the Showmen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair2_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-blair2-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Chantays" title="The Chantays">The Chantays</a> scored a top ten national hit with "<a href="/wiki/Pipeline_(instrumental)" title="Pipeline (instrumental)">Pipeline</a>" in 1963 and probably the best known surf tune was 1963's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wipe_Out_(song)" title="Wipe Out (song)">Wipe Out</a>", by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfaris" title="Surfaris">Surfaris</a>, which hit number 2 and number 10 on the Billboard charts in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="578" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/220px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/330px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/440px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a> performing in 1964</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Groups which crossed over to this genre included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Astronauts_(US_band)" title="The Astronauts (US band)">the Astronauts</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>; <a href="/wiki/The_Trashmen" title="The Trashmen">the Trashmen</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis,_Minnesota" title="Minneapolis, Minnesota">Minneapolis, Minnesota</a>, who had a number 4 hit with "Surfin Bird" in 1964; and <a href="/wiki/The_Rivieras" title="The Rivieras">the Rivieras</a> from <a href="/wiki/South_Bend,_Indiana" title="South Bend, Indiana">South Bend, Indiana</a>, who reached number 5 in 1964 with "California Sun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantics" title="The Atlantics">The Atlantics</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, made a significant contribution to the genre, with their hit "Bombora" (1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup> European instrumental bands around this time generally focused more on the more rock and roll style played by The Shadows, but <a href="/wiki/The_Dakotas_(band)" title="The Dakotas (band)">the Dakotas</a>, who were the British backing band for Merseybeat singer <a href="/wiki/Billy_J._Kramer" title="Billy J. Kramer">Billy J. Kramer</a>, gained some attention as surf musicians with "Cruel Sea" (1963), which was later covered by American instrumental surf bands, including the Ventures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Surf music achieved its greatest commercial success as vocal music, particularly the work of <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">the Beach Boys</a>, formed in 1961 in Southern California. Their early albums included both instrumental surf rock (among them covers of music by Dick Dale) and vocal songs, drawing on rock and roll and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_wop" title="Doo wop">doo wop</a> and the close harmonies of vocal pop acts like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Freshmen" title="Four Freshmen">Four Freshmen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup> Their first chart hit, "<a href="/wiki/Surfin%27" title="Surfin'">Surfin'</a>" in 1962 reached the Billboard top 100 and helped make the surf music craze a national phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Beachboys_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Beachboys-59">[59]</a></sup> From 1963 the group began to leave surfing behind as subject matter as <a href="/wiki/Brian_Wilson" title="Brian Wilson">Brian Wilson</a> became their major composer and producer, moving on to the more general themes of male adolescence including cars and girls in songs like "<a href="/wiki/Fun,_Fun,_Fun" title="Fun, Fun, Fun">Fun, Fun, Fun</a>" (1964) and "<a href="/wiki/California_Girls" title="California Girls">California Girls</a>" (1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Beachboys_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Beachboys-59">[59]</a></sup> Other vocal surf acts followed, including one-hit wonders like <a href="/wiki/Ronny_%26_the_Daytonas" title="Ronny &amp; the Daytonas">Ronny &amp; the Daytonas</a> with "G. T. O." (1964) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rip_Chords" title="Rip Chords">Rip Chords</a> with "<a href="/wiki/Hey_Little_Cobra" title="Hey Little Cobra">Hey Little Cobra</a>", which both reached the top ten, but the only other act to achieve sustained success with the formula were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_%26_Dean" title="Jan &amp; Dean">Jan &amp; Dean</a>, who had a number 1 hit with "Surf City" (co-written with Brian Wilson) in 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup> The surf music craze and the careers of almost all surf acts was effectively ended by the arrival of the British Invasion from 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Surf_55-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Surf-55">[55]</a></sup> Only the Beach Boys were able to sustain a creative career into the mid-1960s, producing a string of hit singles and albums, including the highly regarded <i><a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds" title="Pet Sounds">Pet Sounds</a></i> in 1966, which made them, arguably, the only American rock or pop act that could rival The Beatles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Beachboys_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Beachboys-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Invasion">British Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: British Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">Beat music</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_rock" title="British rock">British rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_in_America.JPG"><img alt="Black and white picture of the Beatles waving in front of a crowd with an set of aeroplane steps in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2526" data-file-width="3578" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/220px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/330px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/440px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_in_America.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> arriving in New York in January 1964 at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the end of 1962, what would become the British rock scene had started with <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">beat groups</a> like the Beatles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerry_%26_the_Pacemakers" title="Gerry &amp; the Pacemakers">Gerry &amp; the Pacemakers</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Searchers_(band)" title="The Searchers (band)">the Searchers</a> from Liverpool and <a href="/wiki/Freddie_and_the_Dreamers" title="Freddie and the Dreamers">Freddie and the Dreamers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herman%27s_Hermits" title="Herman's Hermits">Herman's Hermits</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Hollies" title="The Hollies">the Hollies</a> from Manchester. They drew on a wide range of American influences including soul, rhythm and blues and surf music,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stakes2001_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stakes2001-60">[60]</a></sup> initially reinterpreting standard American tunes and playing for dancers. Bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Animals" title="The Animals">the Animals</a> from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Them_(band)" title="Them (band)">Them</a> from <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and particularly those from London like <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Yardbirds" title="The Yardbirds">the Yardbirds</a>, were much more directly influenced by rhythm and blues and later blues music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Soon these groups were composing their own material, combining US forms of music and infusing it with a high energy beat. Beat bands tended towards "bouncy, irresistible melodies", while early <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a> acts tended towards less sexually innocent, more aggressive songs, often adopting an anti-establishment stance. There was, however, particularly in the early stages, considerable musical crossover between the two tendencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BI_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BI-63">[63]</a></sup> By 1963, led by the Beatles, beat groups had begun to achieve national success in Britain, soon to be followed into the charts by the more rhythm and blues focused acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BritishR.26B_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BritishR.26B-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/I_Want_to_Hold_Your_Hand" title="I Want to Hold Your Hand">I Want to Hold Your Hand</a>" was the Beatles' first number 1 hit on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_28_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_28-65">[65]</a></sup> spending 7 weeks at the top and a total of 15 weeks on the chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaBI_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaBI-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Their first appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show" title="The Ed Sullivan Show">The Ed Sullivan Show</a></i> on 9 February 1964, drawing an estimated 73 million viewers (at the time a record for an American television program) is often considered a milestone in American pop culture. During the week of 4 April 1964, the Beatles held twelve positions on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100">Billboard Hot 100</a> singles chart, including the entire top five. The Beatles went on to become the biggest selling rock band of all time and they were followed into the US charts by numerous British bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BI_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BI-63">[63]</a></sup> During the next two years British acts dominated their own and the US charts with <a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Gordon" title="Peter and Gordon">Peter and Gordon</a>, the Animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Mann" title="Manfred Mann">Manfred Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petula_Clark" title="Petula Clark">Petula Clark</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29-68">[68]</a></sup> Freddie and the Dreamers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wayne_Fontana_and_the_Mindbenders" title="Wayne Fontana and the Mindbenders">Wayne Fontana and the Mindbenders</a>, Herman's Hermits, the Rolling Stones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Troggs" title="The Troggs">the Troggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Donovan" title="Donovan">Donovan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_48_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_48-70">[70]</a></sup> all having one or more number one singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaBI_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaBI-66">[66]</a></sup> Other major acts that were part of the invasion included <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">the Kinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Dave_Clark_Five" title="The Dave Clark Five">the Dave Clark Five</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeatlesArrive_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeatlesArrive-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusicBI_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusicBI-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British Invasion helped internationalize the production of rock and roll, opening the door for subsequent British (and Irish) performers to achieve international success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusicBritpop_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusicBritpop-73">[73]</a></sup> In America it arguably spelled the end of instrumental surf music, vocal girl groups and (for a time) the <a href="/wiki/Teen_idol" title="Teen idol">teen idols</a>, that had dominated the American charts in the late 1950s and 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> It dented the careers of established R&amp;B acts like <a href="/wiki/Fats_Domino" title="Fats Domino">Fats Domino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chubby_Checker" title="Chubby Checker">Chubby Checker</a> and even temporarily derailed the chart success of surviving rock and roll acts, including Elvis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The British Invasion also played a major part in the rise of a distinct genre of rock music, and cemented the primacy of the rock group, based on guitars and drums and producing their own material as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singer-songwriters" title="Singer-songwriters">singer-songwriters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Shuker.2C_2005_p._35_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-R._Shuker.2C_2005_p._35-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Garage_rock">Garage rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Garage rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">Garage rock</a></div>
<p>Garage rock was a raw form of rock music, particularly prevalent in North America in the mid-1960s and so called because of the perception that it was rehearsed in the suburban family garage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Garage rock songs often revolved around the traumas of high school life, with songs about "lying girls" being particularly common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> The lyrics and delivery tended to be more aggressive than was common at the time, often with growled or shouted vocals that dissolved into incoherent screaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005-76">[76]</a></sup> They ranged from crude one-chord music (like <a href="/wiki/The_Seeds" title="The Seeds">the Seeds</a>) to near-studio musician quality (including <a href="/wiki/The_Knickerbockers" title="The Knickerbockers">the Knickerbockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Remains" title="The Remains">the Remains</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Estate_(band)" title="The Fifth Estate (band)">the Fifth Estate</a>). There were also regional variations in many parts of the country with flourishing scenes particularly in California and Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> The Pacific Northwest states of Washington and Oregon had perhaps the most defined regional sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_D-Men_1964.JPG"><img alt="A tinted photograph of five members of the D-Men performing with guitars, drums and keyboards" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1480" data-file-width="1891" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/220px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/330px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/440px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_D-Men_1964.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_D-Men" title="The D-Men">the D-Men</a> (later <a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Estate_(band)" title="The Fifth Estate (band)">The Fifth Estate</a>) in 1964</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The style had been evolving from regional scenes as early as 1958. "Tall Cool One" (1959) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Wailers_(rock_band)" title="The Wailers (rock band)">The Wailers</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Louie_Louie" title="Louie Louie">Louie Louie</a>" by <a href="/wiki/The_Kingsmen" title="The Kingsmen">the Kingsmen</a> (1963) are mainstream examples of the genre in its formative stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> By 1963, garage band singles were creeping into the national charts in greater numbers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Revere_and_the_Raiders" title="Paul Revere and the Raiders">Paul Revere and the Raiders</a> (Boise),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Trashmen" title="The Trashmen">the Trashmen</a> (Minneapolis)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Rivieras" title="The Rivieras">the Rivieras</a> (South Bend, Indiana).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Other influential garage bands, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Sonics" title="The Sonics">the Sonics</a> (Tacoma, Washington), never reached the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In this early period many bands were heavily influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surf_rock" title="Surf rock">surf rock</a> and there was a cross-pollination between garage rock and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frat_rock" title="Frat rock">frat rock</a>, sometimes viewed as merely a subgenre of garage rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British Invasion of 1964–66 greatly influenced garage bands, providing them with a national audience, leading many (often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surf_rock" title="Surf rock">surf</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hot_rod" title="Hot rod">hot rod</a> groups) to adopt a British influence, and encouraging many more groups to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> Thousands of garage bands were extant in the US and Canada during the era and hundreds produced regional hits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> Examples include: "The Witch" by Tacoma's <a href="/wiki/The_Sonics" title="The Sonics">the Sonics</a> (1965), "Where You Gonna Go" by Detroit's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unrelated_Segments" title="Unrelated Segments">Unrelated Segments</a> (1967), "Girl I Got News for You" by Miami's <a href="/wiki/The_Birdwatchers" title="The Birdwatchers">Birdwatchers</a> (1966) and "1–2–5" by Montreal's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Haunted_(Montreal)" title="The Haunted (Montreal)">the Haunted</a>. Despite scores of bands being signed to major or large regional labels, most were commercial failures. It is generally agreed that garage rock peaked both commercially and artistically around 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> By 1968 the style largely disappeared from the national charts and at the local level as amateur musicians faced college, work or the <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">draft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> New styles had evolved to replace garage rock (including <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">blues rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">country rock</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_78-6"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-78">[78]</a></sup> In Detroit, garage rock's legacy remained alive into the early 1970s, with bands such as the <a href="/wiki/MC5" title="MC5">MC5</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">the Stooges</a>, who employed a much more aggressive approach to the form. These bands began to be labelled <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> and are now often seen as <a href="/wiki/Proto-punk" title="Proto-punk">proto-punk</a> or proto-<a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">hard rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G._Thompson.3B_2007.3B_134_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-G._Thompson.3B_2007.3B_134-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pop_rock">Pop rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pop rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pop_rock" title="Pop rock">Pop rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">Pop music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_pop" title="Power pop">Power pop</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_power_pop_artists_and_songs" title="List of power pop artists and songs">List of power pop artists and songs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of Don and Phil Everly on stage with guitars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="730" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/220px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/330px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/440px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">The Everly Brothers</a> in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term <i>pop</i> has been used since the early 20th century to refer to popular music in general, but from the mid-1950s it began to be used for a distinct genre, aimed at a youth market, often characterized as a softer alternative to rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firth2001_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Firth2001-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPR_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPR-88">[88]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the British Invasion, from about 1967, it was increasingly used in opposition to the term rock music, to describe a form that was more commercial, ephemeral and accessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-20">[20]</a></sup> In contrast rock music was seen as focusing on extended works, particularly albums, was often associated with particular sub-cultures (like the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a>), placed an emphasis on artistic values and "authenticity", stressed live performance and instrumental or vocal virtuosity and was often seen as encapsulating progressive developments rather than simply reflecting existing trends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firth2001_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Firth2001-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPR_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPR-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, much pop and rock music has been very similar in sound, instrumentation and even lyrical content. The terms "pop-rock" and "power pop" have been used to describe more commercially successful music that uses elements from, or the form of, rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Pop-rock has been defined as an "upbeat variety of rock music represented by artists such as Elton John, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">the Everly Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart" title="Rod Stewart">Rod Stewart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago_(band)" title="Chicago (band)">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Frampton" title="Peter Frampton">Peter Frampton</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The term <i>power pop</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a> in 1966, but not much used until it was applied to bands like <a href="/wiki/Badfinger" title="Badfinger">Badfinger</a> in the 1970s, who proved some of the most commercially successful of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_period">Classic period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Classic period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blues_rock">Blues rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Blues rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">Blues rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a></div>
<p>Although the first impact of the British Invasion on American popular music was through beat and R&amp;B based acts, the impetus was soon taken up by a second wave of bands that drew their inspiration more directly from American <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>, including the Rolling Stones and the Yardbirds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> British blues musicians of the late 1950s and early 1960s had been inspired by the acoustic playing of figures such as <a href="/wiki/Lead_Belly" title="Lead Belly">Lead Belly</a>, who was a major influence on the Skiffle craze, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Johnson_(musician)" title="Robert Johnson (musician)">Robert Johnson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Increasingly they adopted a loud amplified sound, often centered on the electric guitar, based on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_blues" title="Chicago blues">Chicago blues</a>, particularly after the tour of Britain by <a href="/wiki/Muddy_Waters" title="Muddy Waters">Muddy Waters</a> in 1958, which prompted <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Davies" title="Cyril Davies">Cyril Davies</a> and guitarist <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Korner" title="Alexis Korner">Alexis Korner</a> to form the band <a href="/wiki/Blues_Incorporated" title="Blues Incorporated">Blues Incorporated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> The band involved and inspired many of the figures of the subsequent <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a> boom, including members of the Rolling Stones and <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>, combining blues standards and forms with rock instrumentation and emphasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Eric Clapton with a guitar on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1898" data-file-width="2362" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/220px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/330px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/440px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> performing in Barcelona in 1974</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The other key focus for British blues was around <a href="/wiki/John_Mayall" title="John Mayall">John Mayall</a> who formed the <a href="/wiki/John_Mayall_%26_the_Bluesbreakers" title="John Mayall &amp; the Bluesbreakers">Bluesbreakers</a>, whose members included <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> (after his departure from The Yardbirds) and later <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(musician)" title="Peter Green (musician)">Peter Green</a>. Particularly significant was the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Blues_Breakers_with_Eric_Clapton" title="Blues Breakers with Eric Clapton">Blues Breakers with Eric Clapton</a> (Beano)</i> album (1966), considered one of the seminal British blues recordings and the sound of which was much emulated in both Britain and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Eric Clapton went on to form supergroups Cream, <a href="/wiki/Blind_Faith" title="Blind Faith">Blind Faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Derek_and_the_Dominos" title="Derek and the Dominos">Derek and the Dominos</a>, followed by an extensive solo career that helped bring blues rock into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainstream_(terminology)" title="Mainstream (terminology)">mainstream</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> Green, along with the Bluesbreaker's rhythm section <a href="/wiki/Mick_Fleetwood" title="Mick Fleetwood">Mick Fleetwood</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_McVie" title="John McVie">John McVie</a>, formed Peter Green's <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood_Mac" title="Fleetwood Mac">Fleetwood Mac</a>, who enjoyed some of the greatest commercial success in the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> In the late 1960s <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck" title="Jeff Beck">Jeff Beck</a>, also an alumnus of the Yardbirds, moved blues rock in the direction of heavy rock with his band, <a href="/wiki/The_Jeff_Beck_Group" title="The Jeff Beck Group">the Jeff Beck Group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> The last Yardbirds guitarist was <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Page" title="Jimmy Page">Jimmy Page</a>, who went on to form <i>The New Yardbirds</i> which rapidly became <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>. Many of the songs on their first three albums, and occasionally later in their careers, were expansions on traditional blues songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In America, blues rock had been pioneered in the early 1960s by guitarist <a href="/wiki/Lonnie_Mack" title="Lonnie Mack">Lonnie Mack</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> but the genre began to take off in the mid-1960s as acts developed a sound similar to British blues musicians. Key acts included <a href="/wiki/Paul_Butterfield" title="Paul Butterfield">Paul Butterfield</a> (whose band acted like Mayall's Bluesbreakers in Britain as a starting point for many successful musicians), <a href="/wiki/Canned_Heat" title="Canned Heat">Canned Heat</a>, the early <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Airplane" title="Jefferson Airplane">Jefferson Airplane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Winter" title="Johnny Winter">Johnny Winter</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_J._Geils_Band" title="The J. Geils Band">the J. Geils Band</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> with his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_trios" title="Power trios">power trios</a>, the Jimi Hendrix Experience and <a href="/wiki/Band_of_Gypsys" title="Band of Gypsys">Band of Gypsys</a>, whose guitar virtuosity and showmanship would be among the most emulated of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> Blues rock bands from the southern states, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allman_Brothers_Band" title="Allman Brothers Band">Allman Brothers Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lynyrd_Skynyrd" title="Lynyrd Skynyrd">Lynyrd Skynyrd</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ZZ_Top" title="ZZ Top">ZZ Top</a>, incorporated country elements into their style to produce distinctive <a href="/wiki/Southern_rock" title="Southern rock">Southern rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early blues rock bands often emulated jazz, playing long, involved improvisations, which would later be a major element of progressive rock. From about 1967 bands like Cream and the Jimi Hendrix Experience had moved away from purely blues-based music into <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_music" title="Psychedelic music">psychedelia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amg_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-amg-99">[99]</a></sup> By the 1970s, blues rock had become heavier and more riff-based, exemplified by the work of Led Zeppelin and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a>, and the lines between blues rock and <a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">hard rock</a> "were barely visible",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amg_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-amg-99">[99]</a></sup> as bands began recording rock-style albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amg_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-amg-99">[99]</a></sup> The genre was continued in the 1970s by figures such as <a href="/wiki/George_Thorogood" title="George Thorogood">George Thorogood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Travers" title="Pat Travers">Pat Travers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_95-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-95">[95]</a></sup> but, particularly on the British scene (except perhaps for the advent of groups such as <a href="/wiki/Status_Quo_(band)" title="Status Quo (band)">Status Quo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foghat" title="Foghat">Foghat</a> who moved towards a form of high energy and repetitive <a href="/wiki/Boogie_rock" title="Boogie rock">boogie rock</a>), bands became focused on <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a> innovation, and blues rock began to slip out of the mainstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_rock">Folk rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Folk rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">Folk rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electric_folk" title="Electric folk">Electric folk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Joan Baez and Bob Dylan singing while Dylan plays guitar" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2049" data-file-width="2898" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg/220px-Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg/330px-Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg/440px-Joan_Baez_Bob_Dylan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> in 1963</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the 1960s, the scene that had developed out of the <a href="/wiki/American_folk_music_revival" title="American folk music revival">American folk music revival</a> had grown to a major movement, utilising traditional music and new compositions in a traditional style, usually on acoustic instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2007p95_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2007p95-101">[101]</a></sup> In America the genre was pioneered by figures such as <a href="/wiki/Woody_Guthrie" title="Woody Guthrie">Woody Guthrie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pete_Seeger" title="Pete Seeger">Pete Seeger</a> and often identified with <a href="/wiki/Progressive_folk" title="Progressive folk">progressive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Labour_movement" title="Labour movement">labor politics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2007p95_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2007p95-101">[101]</a></sup> In the early sixties figures such as <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> had come to the fore in this movement as singer-songwriters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2007p72_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2007p72-102">[102]</a></sup> Dylan had begun to reach a mainstream audience with hits including "<a href="/wiki/Blowin%27_in_the_Wind" title="Blowin' in the Wind">Blowin' in the Wind</a>" (1963) and "<a href="/wiki/Masters_of_War" title="Masters of War">Masters of War</a>" (1963), which brought "<a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest songs</a>" to a wider public,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> but, although beginning to influence each other, rock and folk music had remained largely separate genres, often with mutually exclusive audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early attempts to combine elements of folk and rock included the Animals' "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_the_Rising_Sun" title="House of the Rising Sun">House of the Rising Sun</a>" (1964), which was the first commercially successful folk song to be recorded with rock and roll instrumentation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and the Beatles "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_a_Loser" title="I'm a Loser">I'm a Loser</a>" (1964), arguably the first Beatles song to be influenced directly by Dylan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The folk rock movement is usually thought to have taken off with <a href="/wiki/The_Byrds" title="The Byrds">The Byrds</a>' recording of Dylan's "<a href="/wiki/Mr._Tambourine_Man" title="Mr. Tambourine Man">Mr. Tambourine Man</a>" which topped the charts in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup> With members who had been part of the cafe-based folk scene in Los Angeles, the Byrds adopted rock instrumentation, including drums and 12-string <a href="/wiki/Rickenbacker" title="Rickenbacker">Rickenbacker</a> guitars, which became a major element in the sound of the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup> Later that year Dylan adopted electric instruments, much to the <a href="/wiki/Electric_Dylan_controversy" title="Electric Dylan controversy">outrage</a> of many folk purists, with his "<a href="/wiki/Like_a_Rolling_Stone" title="Like a Rolling Stone">Like a Rolling Stone</a>" becoming a US hit single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup> Folk rock particularly took off in California, where it led acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Mamas_%26_the_Papas" title="The Mamas &amp;
