the Papas">The Mamas &amp; the Papas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crosby,_Stills_and_Nash" title="Crosby, Stills and Nash">Crosby, Stills and Nash</a> to move to electric instrumentation, and in New York, where it spawned performers including <a href="/wiki/The_Lovin%27_Spoonful" title="The Lovin' Spoonful">The Lovin' Spoonful</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simon_and_Garfunkel" title="Simon and Garfunkel">Simon and Garfunkel</a>, with the latter's acoustic "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sounds_of_Silence" title="The Sounds of Silence">The Sounds of Silence</a>" (1965) being remixed with rock instruments to be the first of many hits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>These acts directly influenced British performers like Donovan and <a href="/wiki/Fairport_Convention" title="Fairport Convention">Fairport Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup> In 1969 Fairport Convention abandoned their mixture of American covers and Dylan-influenced songs to play traditional English folk music on electric instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> This electric folk was taken up by bands including <a href="/wiki/Pentangle_(band)" title="Pentangle (band)">Pentangle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steeleye_Span" title="Steeleye Span">Steeleye Span</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Albion_Band" title="The Albion Band">The Albion Band</a>, which in turn prompted Irish groups like <a href="/wiki/Horslips" title="Horslips">Horslips</a> and Scottish acts like the <a href="/wiki/JSD_Band" title="JSD Band">JSD Band</a>, Spencer's Feat and later <a href="/wiki/Five_Hand_Reel" title="Five Hand Reel">Five Hand Reel</a>, to use their traditional music to create a brand of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_rock" title="Celtic rock">Celtic rock</a> in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Folk rock reached its peak of commercial popularity in the period 1967–68, before many acts moved off in a variety of directions, including Dylan and the Byrds, who began to develop <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">country rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> However, the hybridization of folk and rock has been seen as having a major influence on the development of rock music, bringing in elements of psychedelia, and helping to develop the ideas of the singer-songwriter, the protest song and concepts of "authenticity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FolkRock_104-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FolkRock-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychedelic_rock">Psychedelic rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Psychedelic rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">Psychedelic rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Raga_rock" title="Raga rock">Raga rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jimi_Hendrix_1967.png"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Jimi Hendrix playing a guitar" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="235" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Jimi_Hendrix_1967.png/170px-Jimi_Hendrix_1967.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Jimi_Hendrix_1967.png 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jimi_Hendrix_1967.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> performing on Dutch TV in 1967</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Psychedelic music's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a>-inspired vibe began in the folk scene, with the New York-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Modal_Rounders" title="Holy Modal Rounders">Holy Modal Rounders</a> using the term in their 1964 recording of "<a href="/wiki/Hesitation_Blues" title="Hesitation Blues">Hesitation Blues</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hicks2000_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks2000-111">[111]</a></sup> The first group to advertise themselves as psychedelic rock were <a href="/wiki/The_13th_Floor_Elevators" title="The 13th Floor Elevators">the 13th Floor Elevators</a> from Texas, at the end of 1965; producing an album that made their direction clear, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Psychedelic_Sounds_of_the_13th_Floor_Elevators" title="The Psychedelic Sounds of the 13th Floor Elevators">The Psychedelic Sounds of the 13th Floor Elevators</a></i> the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hicks2000_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks2000-111">[111]</a></sup> The Beatles introduced many of the major elements of the psychedelic sound to audiences in this period, with "<a href="/wiki/I_Feel_Fine" title="I Feel Fine">I Feel Fine</a>" using guitar feedback; in late 1965 the <i><a href="/wiki/Rubber_Soul" title="Rubber Soul">Rubber Soul</a></i> album included the use of a <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitar</a> on "<a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Wood_(This_Bird_Has_Flown)" title="Norwegian Wood (This Bird Has Flown)">Norwegian Wood</a>" and they employed <a href="/wiki/Backmasking" title="Backmasking">backmasking</a> on their 1966 single B-side "<a href="/wiki/Rain_(Beatles_song)" title="Rain (Beatles song)">Rain</a>" and other tracks that appeared on their <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolver_(The_Beatles_album)" title="Revolver (The Beatles album)">Revolver</a></i> album later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Psychedelic rock particularly took off in California's emerging music scene as groups followed the Byrds from folk to folk rock from 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup> The psychedelic life style had already developed in San Francisco and particularly prominent products of the scene were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Grateful_Dead" title="The Grateful Dead">the Grateful Dead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Country_Joe_and_the_Fish" title="Country Joe and the Fish">Country Joe and the Fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Society_(band)" title="The Great Society (band)">the Great Society</a> and Jefferson Airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_41-42_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_41-42-113">[113]</a></sup> The Byrds rapidly progressed from purely folk rock in 1966 with their single "<a href="/wiki/Eight_Miles_High" title="Eight Miles High">Eight Miles High</a>", widely taken to be a reference to drug use. In Britain, an influential band in the genre were The Yardbirds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup> who, with Jeff Beck as their guitarist, increasingly moved into psychedelic territory, adding up-tempo improvised "rave ups", Gregorian chant and world music influences to songs including "Still I'm Sad" (1965) and "Over Under Sideways Down" (1966).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckley2003Yardbirds_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley2003Yardbirds-114">[114]</a></sup> From 1966 the <a href="/wiki/UK_underground" title="UK underground">UK underground</a> scene based in North London supported new acts including <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traffic_(band)" title="Traffic (band)">Traffic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_Machine" title="Soft Machine">Soft Machine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The same year saw Donovan's folk-influenced hit album <i><a href="/wiki/Sunshine_Superman" title="Sunshine Superman">Sunshine Superman</a></i>, considered one of the first psychedelic pop records, as well as the débuts of blues rock bands Cream and the Jimi Hendrix Experience, whose extended guitar-heavy jams became a key feature of psychedelia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Psychedelic rock reached its apogee in the last years of the decade. 1967 saw the Beatles release their definitive psychedelic statement in <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i>, including the controversial track "<a href="/wiki/Lucy_in_the_Sky_with_Diamonds" title="Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds">Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds</a>" and the Rolling Stones responded later that year with <i><a href="/wiki/Their_Satanic_Majesties_Request" title="Their Satanic Majesties Request">Their Satanic Majesties Request</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup> Pink Floyd produced what is usually seen as their best psychedelic work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn" title="The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">The Piper at the Gates of Dawn</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup> In America the <a href="/wiki/Summer_of_Love" title="Summer of Love">Summer of Love</a> was prefaced by the <a href="/wiki/Human_Be-In" title="Human Be-In">Human Be-In</a> event and reached its peak at the <a href="/wiki/Monterey_Pop_Festival" title="Monterey Pop Festival">Monterey Pop Festival</a>, the latter helping to make major American stars of Jimi Hendrix and the Who, whose single "<a href="/wiki/I_Can_See_for_Miles" title="I Can See for Miles">I Can See for Miles</a>" delved into psychedelic territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Key recordings included Jefferson Airplane's <i><a href="/wiki/Surrealistic_Pillow" title="Surrealistic Pillow">Surrealistic Pillow</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/The_Doors" title="The Doors">the Doors</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Days_(album)" title="Strange Days (album)">Strange Days</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> These trends climaxed in the 1969 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodstock_festival" title="Woodstock festival">Woodstock festival</a>, which saw performances by most of the major psychedelic acts, but by the end of the decade psychedelic rock was in retreat. Brian Wilson of the Beach Boys, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Jones" title="Brian Jones">Brian Jones</a> of the Rolling Stones, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(musician)" title="Peter Green (musician)">Peter Green</a> of Fleetwood Mac and <a href="/wiki/Syd_Barrett" title="Syd Barrett">Syd Barrett</a> of Pink Floyd were early "acid casualties", the Jimi Hendrix Experience and Cream broke up before the end of the decade and many surviving acts moved away from psychedelia into more back-to-basics "roots rock", the wider experimentation of progressive rock, or riff-laden heavy rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-7"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progressive_rock">Progressive rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Progressive rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">Progressive rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Art_rock" title="Art rock">Art rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experimental_rock" title="Experimental rock">Experimental rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Progressive_music" title="Progressive music">Progressive music</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section should include only a <strong>brief</strong> summary of <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">Progressive rock</a>.</b> See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">Wikipedia:Summary style</a> for information on how to properly incorporate it into this article's main text. <small><i>(August 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Progressive rock, a term sometimes used interchangeably with <a href="/wiki/Art_rock" title="Art rock">art rock</a>, moved beyond established musical formulas by experimenting with different instruments, song types, and forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> From the mid-1960s <a href="/wiki/The_Left_Banke" title="The Left Banke">the Left Banke</a>, the Beatles, the Rolling Stones and the Beach Boys, had pioneered the inclusion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harpsichords" title="Harpsichords">harpsichords</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_instruments" title="Wind instruments">wind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/String_orchestra" title="String orchestra">string</a> sections on their recordings to produce a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baroque_rock" title="Baroque rock">Baroque rock</a> and can be heard in singles like <a href="/wiki/Procol_Harum" title="Procol Harum">Procol Harum</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/A_Whiter_Shade_of_Pale" title="A Whiter Shade of Pale">A Whiter Shade of Pale</a>" (1967), with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bach" title="Bach">Bach</a>-inspired introduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington2003_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington2003-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Moody_Blues" title="The Moody Blues">The Moody Blues</a> used a full orchestra on their album <i><a href="/wiki/Days_of_Future_Passed" title="Days of Future Passed">Days of Future Passed</a></i> (1967) and subsequently created orchestral sounds with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesisers" title="Synthesisers">synthesisers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> Classical orchestration, keyboards and synthesisers were a frequent addition to the established rock format of guitars, bass and drums in subsequent progressive rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yes_concert.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the band Yes on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1514" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Yes_concert.jpg/250px-Yes_concert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Yes_concert.jpg/375px-Yes_concert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Yes_concert.jpg/500px-Yes_concert.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yes_concert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prog-rock band <a href="/wiki/Yes_(band)" title="Yes (band)">Yes</a> performing in concert in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a> in 1977</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Instrumentals were common, while songs with lyrics were sometimes conceptual, abstract, or based in <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Pretty_Things" title="The Pretty Things">The Pretty Things</a>' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SF_Sorrow" title="SF Sorrow">SF Sorrow</a></i> (1968), the Who's <i><a href="/wiki/Tommy_(album)" title="Tommy (album)">Tommy</a></i> (1969) and the Kinks' <i><a href="/wiki/Arthur_(Or_the_Decline_and_Fall_of_the_British_Empire)" title="Arthur (Or the Decline and Fall of the British Empire)">Arthur (Or the Decline and Fall of the British Empire)</a></i> (1969) introduced the format of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_operas" title="Rock operas">rock operas</a> and opened the door to <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept albums</a>, often telling an epic story or tackling a grand overarching theme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_Crimson" title="King Crimson">King Crimson</a>'s 1969 début album, <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Court_of_the_Crimson_King" title="In the Court of the Crimson King">In the Court of the Crimson King</a></i>, which mixed powerful guitar riffs and <a href="/wiki/Mellotron" title="Mellotron">mellotron</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphonic_music" title="Symphonic music">symphonic music</a>, is often taken as the key recording in progressive rock, helping the widespread adoption of the genre in the early 1970s among existing blues-rock and psychedelic bands, as well as newly formed acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>The vibrant <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_scene" title="Canterbury scene">Canterbury scene</a> saw acts following Soft Machine from psychedelia, through jazz influences, toward more expansive hard rock, including <a href="/wiki/Caravan_(band)" title="Caravan (band)">Caravan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_and_the_North" title="Hatfield and the North">Hatfield and the North</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gong_(band)" title="Gong (band)">Gong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Health" title="National Health">National Health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Greater commercial success was enjoyed by Pink Floyd, who also moved away from psychedelia after the departure of Syd Barrett in 1968, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Side_of_the_Moon" title="The Dark Side of the Moon">The Dark Side of the Moon</a></i> (1973), seen as a masterpiece of the genre, becoming one of the best-selling albums of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> There was an emphasis on instrumental virtuosity, with <a href="/wiki/Yes_(band)" title="Yes (band)">Yes</a> showcasing the skills of both guitarist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steve_Howe_(guitarist)" title="Steve Howe (guitarist)">Steve Howe</a> and keyboard player <a href="/wiki/Rick_Wakeman" title="Rick Wakeman">Rick Wakeman</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Emerson,_Lake_%26_Palmer" title="Emerson, Lake &amp; Palmer">Emerson, Lake &amp; Palmer</a> were a supergroup who produced some of the genre's most technically demanding work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jethro_Tull_(band)" title="Jethro Tull (band)">Jethro Tull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(band)" title="Genesis (band)">Genesis</a> both pursued very different, but distinctly English, brands of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_(band)" title="Renaissance (band)">Renaissance</a>, formed in 1969 by ex-Yardbirds Jim McCarty and Keith Relf, evolved into a high-concept band featuring the three-octave voice of <a href="/wiki/Annie_Haslam" title="Annie Haslam">Annie Haslam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Most British bands depended on a relatively small cult following, but a handful, including Pink Floyd, Genesis and Jethro Tull, managed to produce top ten singles at home and break the American market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>The American brand of progressive rock varied from the eclectic and innovative <a href="/wiki/Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa">Frank Zappa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Captain_Beefheart" title="Captain Beefheart">Captain Beefheart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood,_Sweat_%26_Tears" title="Blood, Sweat &amp; Tears">Blood, Sweat &amp; Tears</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> to more pop rock orientated bands like <a href="/wiki/Boston_(band)" title="Boston (band)">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreigner_(band)" title="Foreigner (band)">Foreigner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_(band)" title="Kansas (band)">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Journey_(band)" title="Journey (band)">Journey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Styx_(band)" title="Styx (band)">Styx</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> These, beside British bands <a href="/wiki/Supertramp" title="Supertramp">Supertramp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_Light_Orchestra" title="Electric Light Orchestra">ELO</a>, all demonstrated a prog rock influence and while ranking among the most commercially successful acts of the 1970s, issuing in the era of <i>pomp</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Arena_rock" title="Arena rock">arena rock</a></i>, which would last until the costs of complex shows (often with theatrical staging and special effects), would be replaced by more economical <a href="/wiki/Rock_festival" title="Rock festival">rock festivals</a> as major live venues in the 1990s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The instrumental strand of the genre resulted in albums like <a href="/wiki/Mike_Oldfield" title="Mike Oldfield">Mike Oldfield</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubular_Bells_(album)" title="Tubular Bells (album)">Tubular Bells</a></i> (1973), the first record, and worldwide hit, for the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records</a> label, which became a mainstay of the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> Instrumental rock was particularly significant in continental Europe, allowing bands like <a href="/wiki/Kraftwerk" title="Kraftwerk">Kraftwerk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tangerine_Dream" title="Tangerine Dream">Tangerine Dream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Can_(band)" title="Can (band)">Can</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Faust_(band)" title="Faust (band)">Faust</a> to circumvent the language barrier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Their synthesiser-heavy "<a href="/wiki/Krautrock" title="Krautrock">krautrock</a>", along with the work of <a href="/wiki/Brian_Eno" title="Brian Eno">Brian Eno</a> (for a time the keyboard player with <a href="/wiki/Roxy_Music" title="Roxy Music">Roxy Music</a>), would be a major influence on subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">electronic rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Prog_118-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Prog-118">[118]</a></sup> With the advent of punk rock and technological changes in the late 1970s, progressive rock was increasingly dismissed as pretentious and overblown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Many bands broke up, but some, including Genesis, ELP, Yes, and Pink Floyd, regularly scored top ten albums with successful accompanying worldwide tours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G._Thompson.3B_2007.3B_134_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-G._Thompson.3B_2007.3B_134-86">[86]</a></sup> Some bands which emerged in the aftermath of punk, such as <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_and_the_Banshees" title="Siouxsie and the Banshees">Siouxsie and the Banshees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultravox" title="Ultravox">Ultravox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Simple_Minds" title="Simple Minds">Simple Minds</a>, showed the influence of progressive rock, as well as their more usually recognized punk influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClassicRock_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClassicRock-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roots_rock">Roots rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Roots rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roots_rock" title="Roots rock">Roots rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">Country rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_rock" title="Southern rock">Southern rock</a></div>
<p>Roots rock is the term now used to describe a move away from what some saw as the excesses of the psychedelic scene, to a more basic form of rock and roll that incorporated its original influences, particularly country and folk music, leading to the creation of country rock and Southern rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 1966 Bob Dylan went to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nashville" title="Nashville">Nashville</a> to record the album <i><a href="/wiki/Blonde_on_Blonde" title="Blonde on Blonde">Blonde on Blonde</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff2000_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2000-134">[134]</a></sup> This, and subsequent more clearly country-influenced albums, have been seen as creating the genre of <a href="/wiki/Country_folk" title="Country folk">country folk</a>, a route pursued by a number of largely acoustic folk musicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff2000_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2000-134">[134]</a></sup> Other acts that followed the back-to-basics trend were the Canadian group <a href="/wiki/The_Band" title="The Band">the Band</a> and the California-based <a href="/wiki/Creedence_Clearwater_Revival" title="Creedence Clearwater Revival">Creedence Clearwater Revival</a>, both of which mixed basic rock and roll with folk, country and blues, to be among the most successful and influential bands of the late 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The same movement saw the beginning of the recording careers of Californian solo artists like <a href="/wiki/Ry_Cooder" title="Ry Cooder">Ry Cooder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Raitt" title="Bonnie Raitt">Bonnie Raitt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lowell_George" title="Lowell George">Lowell George</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and influenced the work of established performers such as the Rolling Stones' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beggar%27s_Banquet" title="Beggar's Banquet">Beggar's Banquet</a></i> (1968) and the Beatles' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Let_It_Be_(The_Beatles_album)" title="Let It Be (The Beatles album)">Let It Be</a></i> (1970).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Psych_112-8"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Psych-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eagles.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of four members of the Eagles on stage with guitars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eagles.jpg/220px-Eagles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eagles.jpg/330px-Eagles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eagles.jpg/440px-Eagles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eagles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles (band)</a> during their 2008–2009 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_Road_out_of_Eden_Tour" title="Long Road out of Eden Tour">Long Road out of Eden Tour</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1968 <a href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons" title="Gram Parsons">Gram Parsons</a> recorded <i><a href="/wiki/Safe_at_Home" title="Safe at Home">Safe at Home</a></i> with the <a href="/wiki/International_Submarine_Band" title="International Submarine Band">International Submarine Band</a>, arguably the first true <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">country rock</a> album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> Later that year he joined the Byrds for <i><a href="/wiki/Sweetheart_of_the_Rodeo" title="Sweetheart of the Rodeo">Sweetheart of the Rodeo</a></i> (1968), generally considered one of the most influential recordings in the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> The Byrds continued in the same vein, but Parsons left to be joined by another ex-Byrds member <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hillman" title="Chris Hillman">Chris Hillman</a> in forming <a href="/wiki/The_Flying_Burrito_Brothers" title="The Flying Burrito Brothers">the Flying Burrito Brothers</a> who helped establish the respectability and parameters of the genre, before Parsons departed to pursue a solo career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> Bands in California that adopted country rock included Hearts and Flowers, <a href="/wiki/Poco" title="Poco">Poco</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Riders_of_the_Purple_Sage" title="New Riders of the Purple Sage">New Riders of the Purple Sage</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beau_Brummels" title="Beau Brummels">Beau Brummels</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nitty_Gritty_Dirt_Band" title="Nitty Gritty Dirt Band">Nitty Gritty Dirt Band</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Some performers also enjoyed a renaissance by adopting country sounds, including: the Everly Brothers; one-time <a href="/wiki/Teen_idol" title="Teen idol">teen idol</a> Rick Nelson who became the frontman for the Stone Canyon Band; former Monkee <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mike_Nesmith" title="Mike Nesmith">Mike Nesmith</a> who formed the <a href="/wiki/First_National_Band" title="First National Band">First National Band</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Dillards" title="The Dillards">The Dillards</a> were, unusually, a country act, who moved towards rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002C-Rock_137-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002C-Rock-137">[137]</a></sup> The greatest commercial success for country rock came in the 1970s, with artists including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doobie_Brothers" title="Doobie Brothers">Doobie Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmylou_Harris" title="Emmylou Harris">Emmylou Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt" title="Linda Ronstadt">Linda Ronstadt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles</a> (made up of members of the Burritos, Poco, and Stone Canyon Band), who emerged as one of the most successful rock acts of all time, producing albums that included <i><a href="/wiki/Hotel_California_(Eagles_album)" title="Hotel California (Eagles album)">Hotel California</a></i> (1976).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tawa2005_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tawa2005-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The founders of Southern rock are usually thought to be the Allman Brothers Band, who developed a distinctive sound, largely derived from <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">blues rock</a>, but incorporating elements of <a href="/wiki/Boogie" title="Boogie">boogie</a>, soul, and country in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock-98">[98]</a></sup> The most successful act to follow them were Lynyrd Skynyrd, who helped establish the "<a href="/wiki/Good_ol%27_boy" title="Good ol' boy">Good ol' boy</a>" image of the subgenre and the general shape of 1970s' guitar rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock-98">[98]</a></sup> Their successors included the fusion/progressive instrumentalists <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Dregs" title="Dixie Dregs">Dixie Dregs</a>, the more country-influenced <a href="/wiki/Outlaws_(band)" title="Outlaws (band)">Outlaws</a>, jazz-leaning <a href="/wiki/Wet_Willie" title="Wet Willie">Wet Willie</a> and (incorporating elements of R&amp;B and gospel) the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozark_Mountain_Daredevils" title="Ozark Mountain Daredevils">Ozark Mountain Daredevils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock-98">[98]</a></sup> After the loss of original members of the Allmans and Lynyrd Skynyrd, the genre began to fade in popularity in the late 1970s, but was sustained the 1980s with acts like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/.38_Special_(band)" title=".38 Special (band)">.38 Special</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molly_Hatchet" title="Molly Hatchet">Molly Hatchet</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Marshall_Tucker_Band" title="The Marshall Tucker Band">the Marshall Tucker Band</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002SouthernRock-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jazz_rock">Jazz rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Jazz rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_rock" title="Jazz rock">Jazz rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of Jaco Pastorius sitting on a stool and playing a bass guitar" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="683" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg/150px-Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg/225px-Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg/300px-Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jaco-Pastorius_seated_1980.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jaco_Pastorius" title="Jaco Pastorius">Jaco Pastorius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Weather_Report" title="Weather Report">Weather Report</a> in 1980</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the late 1960s jazz rock emerged as a distinct subgenre out of the blues rock, psychedelic and progressive rock scenes, mixing the power of rock with the musical complexity and improvisational elements of jazz. <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> states that the term jazz-rock "may refer to the loudest, wildest, most electrified fusion bands from the jazz camp, but most often it describes performers coming from the rock side of the equation." Jazz rock "...generally grew out of the most artistically ambitious rock subgenres of the late '60s and early '70s", including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singer/songwriter" title="Singer/songwriter">singer/songwriter</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic.com_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic.com-140">[140]</a></sup> Many early US rock and roll musicians had begun in jazz and carried some of these elements into the new music. In Britain the subgenre of blues rock, and many of its leading figures, like <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Baker" title="Ginger Baker">Ginger Baker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bruce" title="Jack Bruce">Jack Bruce</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a>-fronted band <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>, had emerged from the <a href="/wiki/British_jazz" title="British jazz">British jazz</a> scene. Often highlighted as the first true jazz-rock recording is the only album by the relatively obscure New York-based <a href="/wiki/The_Free_Spirits" title="The Free Spirits">the Free Spirits</a> with <i>Out of Sight and Sound</i> (1966). The first group of bands to self-consciously use the label were R&amp;B oriented white rock bands that made use of jazzy horn sections, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_Flag" title="Electric Flag">Electric Flag</a>, Blood, Sweat &amp; Tears and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_(band)" title="Chicago (band)">Chicago</a>, to become some of the most commercially successful acts of the later 1960s and early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002JazzRock_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002JazzRock-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>British acts to emerge in the same period from the blues scene, to make use of the tonal and improvisational aspects of jazz, included <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_(band)" title="Nucleus (band)">Nucleus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Graham_Bond" title="Graham Bond">Graham Bond</a> and John Mayall spin-off <a href="/wiki/Colosseum_(band)" title="Colosseum (band)">Colosseum</a>. From the psychedelic rock and the Canterbury scenes came Soft Machine, who, it has been suggested, produced one of the artistically successfully fusions of the two genres. Perhaps the most critically acclaimed fusion came from the jazz side of the equation, with <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a>, particularly influenced by the work of Hendrix, incorporating rock instrumentation into his sound for the album <i><a href="/wiki/Bitches_Brew" title="Bitches Brew">Bitches Brew</a></i> (1970). It was a major influence on subsequent rock-influenced jazz artists, including <a href="/wiki/Herbie_Hancock" title="Herbie Hancock">Herbie Hancock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chick_Corea" title="Chick Corea">Chick Corea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_Report" title="Weather Report">Weather Report</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002JazzRock_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002JazzRock-141">[141]</a></sup> The genre began to fade in the late 1970s, as a mellower form of fusion began to take its audience,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMJazzRock_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMJazzRock-143">[143]</a></sup> but acts like <a href="/wiki/Steely_Dan" title="Steely Dan">Steely Dan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMJazzRock_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMJazzRock-143">[143]</a></sup> Frank Zappa and <a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a> recorded significant jazz-influenced albums in this period, and it has continued to be a major influence on rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002JazzRock_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002JazzRock-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glam_rock">Glam rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Glam rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">Glam rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David-Bowie_Early.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of David Bowie with an acoustic guitar" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="302" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg/170px-David-Bowie_Early.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg/255px-David-Bowie_Early.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David-Bowie_Early.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a> during the <a href="/wiki/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_Ziggy_Stardust_and_the_Spiders_from_Mars" title="The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars">Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders</a> Tour in 1972</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Glam rock emerged from the English psychedelic and art rock scenes of the late 1960s and can be seen as both an extension of and reaction against those trends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005pp124-5_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005pp124-5-144">[144]</a></sup> Musically diverse, varying between the simple rock and roll revivalism of figures like <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Stardust" title="Alvin Stardust">Alvin Stardust</a> to the complex art rock of Roxy Music, and can be seen as much as a fashion as a musical subgenre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005pp124-5_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005pp124-5-144">[144]</a></sup> Visually it was a mesh of various styles, ranging from 1930s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinema_in_the_United_States" title="Cinema in the United States">Hollywood</a> glamor, through 1950s pin-up sex appeal, pre-war <a href="/wiki/Cabaret" title="Cabaret">Cabaret</a> theatrics, <a href="/wiki/Victorian_literature" title="Victorian literature">Victorian</a> literary and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbolist" title="Symbolist">symbolist</a> styles, <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, to ancient and occult <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>; manifesting itself in outrageous clothes, makeup, hairstyles, and platform-soled boots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Glam is most noted for its sexual and gender ambiguity and representations of <a href="/wiki/Androgyny" title="Androgyny">androgyny</a>, beside extensive use of theatrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGR_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGR-146">[146]</a></sup> It was prefigured by the showmanship and gender-identity manipulation of American acts such as <a href="/wiki/The_Cockettes" title="The Cockettes">the Cockettes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">Alice Cooper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>The origins of glam rock are associated with <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bolan" title="Marc Bolan">Marc Bolan</a>, who had renamed his folk duo to <a href="/wiki/T._Rex_(band)" title="T. Rex (band)">T. Rex</a> and taken up electric instruments by the end of the 1960s. Often cited as the moment of inception is his appearance on the UK TV programme <i><a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">Top of the Pops</a></i> in December 1970 wearing glitter, to perform what would be his first number 1 single "<a href="/wiki/Ride_a_White_Swan" title="Ride a White Swan">Ride a White Swan</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> From 1971, already a minor star, <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a> developed his Ziggy Stardust persona, incorporating elements of professional make up, mime and performance into his act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p72_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p72-149">[149]</a></sup> These performers were soon followed in the style by acts including Roxy Music, <a href="/wiki/The_Sweet" title="The Sweet">Sweet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slade" title="Slade">Slade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mott_the_Hoople" title="Mott the Hoople">Mott the Hoople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mud_(band)" title="Mud (band)">Mud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Stardust" title="Alvin Stardust">Alvin Stardust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p72_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p72-149">[149]</a></sup> While highly successful in the single charts in the UK, very few of these musicians were able to make a serious impact in the United States; Bowie was the major exception becoming an international superstar and prompting the adoption of glam styles among acts like <a href="/wiki/Lou_Reed" title="Lou Reed">Lou Reed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Pop" title="Iggy Pop">Iggy Pop</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_Dolls" title="New York Dolls">New York Dolls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jobriath" title="Jobriath">Jobriath</a>, often known as "glitter rock" and with a darker lyrical content than their British counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p80_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p80-150">[150]</a></sup> In the UK the term glitter rock was most often used to refer to the extreme version of glam pursued by <a href="/wiki/Gary_Glitter" title="Gary Glitter">Gary Glitter</a> and his support musicians the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glitter_Band" title="Glitter Band">Glitter Band</a>, who between them achieved eighteen top ten singles in the UK between 1972 and 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Glitter_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Glitter-151">[151]</a></sup> A second wave of glam rock acts, including <a href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro" title="Suzi Quatro">Suzi Quatro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Wood" title="Roy Wood">Roy Wood</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Wizzard" title="Wizzard">Wizzard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparks_(band)" title="Sparks (band)">Sparks</a>, dominated the British single charts from about 1974 to 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p72_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p72-149">[149]</a></sup> Existing acts, some not usually considered central to the genre, also adopted glam styles, including <a href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart" title="Rod Stewart">Rod Stewart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a> and, for a time, even the Rolling Stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p72_149-3"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p72-149">[149]</a></sup> It was also a direct influence on acts that rose to prominence later, including <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ant" title="Adam Ant">Adam Ant</a>, and less directly on the formation of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_rock" title="Gothic rock">gothic rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">glam metal</a> as well as on punk rock, which helped end the fashion for glam from about 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2006p80_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2006p80-150">[150]</a></sup> Glam has since enjoyed sporadic modest revivals through bands such as <a href="/wiki/Chainsaw_Kittens" title="Chainsaw Kittens">Chainsaw Kittens</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Darkness_(band)" title="The Darkness (band)">the Darkness</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> and in R n' B crossover act <a href="/wiki/Prince_(musician)" title="Prince (musician)">Prince</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_rock.2C_hard_rock_and_early_heavy_metal">Soft rock, hard rock and early heavy metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Soft rock, hard rock and early heavy metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Soft_rock" title="Soft rock">Soft rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">Hard rock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal music</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_soft_rock_artists_and_songs" title="List of soft rock artists and songs">List of soft rock artists and songs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of the band Led Zeppelin on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/220px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/330px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/440px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a> live at <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Stadium" title="Chicago Stadium">Chicago Stadium</a> in January 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the late 1960s it became common to divide mainstream rock music into soft and hard rock. Soft rock was often derived from folk rock, using acoustic instruments and putting more emphasis on melody and harmonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtis1987_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curtis1987-154">[154]</a></sup> Major artists included <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cat_Stevens" title="Cat Stevens">Cat Stevens</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Taylor" title="James Taylor">James Taylor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtis1987_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curtis1987-154">[154]</a></sup> It reached its commercial peak in the mid- to late 1970s with acts like <a href="/wiki/Billy_Joel" title="Billy Joel">Billy Joel</a>, <a href="/wiki/America_(band)" title="America (band)">America</a> and the reformed <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood_Mac" title="Fleetwood Mac">Fleetwood Mac</a>, whose <i><a href="/wiki/Rumours_(album)" title="Rumours (album)">Rumours</a></i> (1977) was the best-selling album of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> In contrast, hard rock was more often derived from blues-rock and was played louder and with more intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicHard_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicHard-156">[156]</a></sup> It often emphasised the electric guitar, both as a rhythm instrument using simple repetitive riffs and as a solo <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">lead</a> instrument, and was more likely to be used with <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">distortion</a> and other effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicHard_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicHard-156">[156]</a></sup> Key acts included British Invasion bands like the Who and the Kinks, as well as psychedelic era performers like Cream, Jimi Hendrix and <a href="/wiki/The_Jeff_Beck_Group" title="The Jeff Beck Group">the Jeff Beck Group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicHard_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicHard-156">[156]</a></sup> Hard rock-influenced bands that enjoyed international success in the later 1970s included Queen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy" title="Thin Lizzy">Thin Lizzy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith" title="Aerosmith">Aerosmith</a> and <a href="/wiki/AC/DC" title="AC/DC">AC/DC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicHard_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicHard-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the late 1960s the term "heavy metal" began to be used to describe some hard rock played with even more volume and intensity, first as an adjective and by the early 1970s as a noun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The term was first used in music in <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">Steppenwolf</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Born_to_Be_Wild" title="Born to Be Wild">Born to Be Wild</a>" (1967) and began to be associated with pioneer bands like San Francisco's <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">Blue Cheer</a> and Michigan's <a href="/wiki/Grand_Funk_Railroad" title="Grand Funk Railroad">Grand Funk Railroad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> By 1970 three key British bands had developed the characteristic sounds and styles which would help shape the subgenre. <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a> added elements of <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> to their riff laden blues-rock, <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a> brought in symphonic and medieval interests from their progressive rock phase and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a> introduced facets of the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_rock" title="Gothic rock">gothic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_mode" title="Musical mode">modal harmony</a>, helping to produce a "darker" sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser1993p10_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walser1993p10-161">[161]</a></sup> These elements were taken up by a "second generation" of heavy metal bands into the late 1970s, including: <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a>, <a href="/wiki/UFO_(band)" title="UFO (band)">UFO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Motörhead">Motörhead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(rock_band)" title="Rainbow (rock band)">Rainbow</a> from Britain; <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Nugent" title="Ted Nugent">Ted Nugent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blue_%C3%96yster_Cult" title="Blue Öyster Cult">Blue Öyster Cult</a> from the US; <a href="/wiki/Rush_(band)" title="Rush (band)">Rush</a> from Canada and <a href="/wiki/Scorpions_(band)" title="Scorpions (band)">Scorpions</a> from Germany, all marking the expansion in popularity of the subgenre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser1993p10_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walser1993p10-161">[161]</a></sup> Despite a lack of airplay and very little presence on the singles charts, late-1970s heavy metal built a considerable following, particularly among adolescent working-class males in North America and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_rock">Christian rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Christian rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_rock" title="Christian rock">Christian rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of the band Stryper on stage under a large cross of lights" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="532" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg/220px-Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg/330px-Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg/440px-Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stryper_Concert_1986.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stryper" title="Stryper">Stryper</a> on stage in 1986</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rock, mostly the heavy metal genre, has been criticized by some <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> leaders, who have condemned it as immoral, anti-Christian and even demonic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> However, Christian rock began to develop in the late 1960s, particularly out of the <a href="/wiki/Jesus_movement" title="Jesus movement">Jesus movement</a> beginning in Southern California, and emerged as a subgenre in the 1970s with artists like <a href="/wiki/Larry_Norman" title="Larry Norman">Larry Norman</a>, usually seen as the first major "star" of Christian rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The genre has been particularly popular in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Many Christian rock performers have ties to the <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Christian_music" title="Contemporary Christian music">contemporary Christian music</a> scene, while other bands and artists are closely linked to <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">independent music</a>. Since the 1980s Christian rock performers have gained mainstream success, including figures such as the American gospel-to-pop crossover artist <a href="/wiki/Amy_Grant" title="Amy Grant">Amy Grant</a> and the British singer <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Richard" title="Cliff Richard">Cliff Richard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> While these artists were largely acceptable in Christian communities the adoption of heavy rock and glam metal styles by bands like <a href="/wiki/Petra_(band)" title="Petra (band)">Petra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stryper" title="Stryper">Stryper</a>, who achieved considerable mainstream success in the 1980s, was more controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> From the 1990s there were increasing numbers of acts who attempted to avoid the Christian band label, preferring to be seen as groups who were also Christians, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P.O.D" title="P.O.D">P.O.D</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collective_Soul" title="Collective Soul">Collective Soul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Punk_era">Punk era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Punk era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Punk_rock">Punk rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Punk rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">Punk rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protopunk" title="Protopunk">Protopunk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">Hardcore punk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of Patti Smith on stage with a microphone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="1305" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg/170px-Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg/255px-Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg/340px-Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patti_Smith_Copenhagen_1976.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Patti_Smith" title="Patti Smith">Patti Smith</a>, performing in 1976</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Punk rock was developed between 1974 and 1976 in the United States and the United Kingdom. Rooted in garage rock and other forms of what is now known as protopunk music, punk rock bands eschewed the perceived excesses of mainstream 1970s rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BogdanovPunk_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-BogdanovPunk-170">[170]</a></sup> They created fast, hard-edged music, typically with short songs, stripped-down instrumentation, and often political, anti-establishment lyrics. Punk embraces a <a href="/wiki/DIY_ethic" title="DIY ethic">DIY (do it yourself) ethic</a>, with many bands self-producing their recordings and distributing them through informal channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>By late 1976, acts such as the <a href="/wiki/Ramones" title="Ramones">Ramones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patti_Smith" title="Patti Smith">Patti Smith</a>, in New York City, and the <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">the Clash</a>, in London, were recognized as the vanguard of a new musical movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BogdanovPunk_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-BogdanovPunk-170">[170]</a></sup> The following year saw punk rock spreading around the world. Punk quickly, though briefly, became a major cultural phenomenon in the United Kingdom. For the most part, punk took root in local scenes that tended to reject association with the mainstream. An associated <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk subculture</a> emerged, expressing youthful rebellion and characterized by distinctive <a href="/wiki/Punk_fashion" title="Punk fashion">clothing styles</a> and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Punk_ideologies" title="Punk ideologies">anti-authoritarian ideologies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the beginning of the 1980s, faster, more aggressive styles such as hardcore and <a href="/wiki/Oi!" title="Oi!">Oi!</a> had become the predominant mode of punk rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> This has resulted in several evolved strains of hardcore punk, such as <a href="/wiki/D-beat" title="D-beat">D-beat</a> (a distortion-heavy subgenre influenced by the UK band <a href="/wiki/Discharge_(band)" title="Discharge (band)">Discharge</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-punk" title="Anarcho-punk">anarcho-punk</a> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crass_(band)" title="Crass (band)">Crass</a>), <a href="/wiki/Grindcore" title="Grindcore">grindcore</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Napalm_Death" title="Napalm Death">Napalm Death</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Crust_punk" title="Crust punk">crust punk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Musicians identifying with or inspired by punk also pursued a broad range of other variations, giving rise to <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">New wave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-punk" title="Post-punk">post-punk</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BogdanovPunk_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-BogdanovPunk-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_wave">New wave</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: New wave">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">New wave music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">Synthpop</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Romanticism" title="New Romanticism">New Romanticism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">Electronic rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blondie_(Debbie_Harry)_One.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Debbie Harry on stage with a microphone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="537" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg/220px-Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg/330px-Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg/440px-Blondie_%28Debbie_Harry%29_One.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blondie_(Debbie_Harry)_One.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Debbie_Harry" title="Debbie Harry">Deborah Harry</a> from the band <a href="/wiki/Blondie_(band)" title="Blondie (band)">Blondie</a>, performing at Maple Leaf Gardens in Toronto in 1977</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although punk rock was a significant social and musical phenomenon, it achieved less in the way of record sales (being distributed by small specialty labels such as <a href="/wiki/Stiff_Records" title="Stiff Records">Stiff Records</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> or American radio airplay (as the radio scene continued to be dominated by mainstream formats such as <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Album-oriented_rock" title="Album-oriented rock">album-oriented rock</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Punk rock had attracted devotees from the art and collegiate world and soon bands sporting a more literate, arty approach, such as <a href="/wiki/Talking_Heads" title="Talking Heads">Talking Heads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devo" title="Devo">Devo</a> began to infiltrate the punk scene; in some quarters the description "new wave" began to be used to differentiate these less overtly punk bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Record executives, who had been mostly mystified by the punk movement, recognized the potential of the more accessible new wave acts and began aggressively signing and marketing any band that could claim a remote connection to punk or new wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Many of these bands, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Cars" title="The Cars">the Cars</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Go-Go%27s" title="The Go-Go's">the Go-Go's</a> can be seen as pop bands marketed as new wave;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> other existing acts, including <a href="/wiki/The_Police" title="The Police">the Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Pretenders" title="The Pretenders">the Pretenders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Costello" title="Elvis Costello">Elvis Costello</a>, used the new wave movement as the springboard for relatively long and critically successful careers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> while "skinny tie" bands exemplified by <a href="/wiki/The_Knack" title="The Knack">the Knack</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> or the photogenic <a href="/wiki/Blondie_(band)" title="Blondie (band)">Blondie</a>, began as punk acts and moved into more commercial territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002NewWave_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002NewWave-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1979 and 1985, influenced by Kraftwerk, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Magic_Orchestra" title="Yellow Magic Orchestra">Yellow Magic Orchestra</a>, David Bowie and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Numan" title="Gary Numan">Gary Numan</a>, British new wave went in the direction of such New Romantics as <a href="/wiki/Spandau_Ballet" title="Spandau Ballet">Spandau Ballet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultravox" title="Ultravox">Ultravox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan_(band)" title="Japan (band)">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duran_Duran" title="Duran Duran">Duran Duran</a>, <a href="/wiki/A_Flock_of_Seagulls" title="A Flock of Seagulls">A Flock of Seagulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_Club" title="Culture Club">Culture Club</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talk_Talk" title="Talk Talk">Talk Talk</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurythmics" title="Eurythmics">Eurythmics</a>, sometimes using the synthesizer to replace all other instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borthwick.26Moy2004pp121-3_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borthwick.26Moy2004pp121-3-183">[183]</a></sup> This period coincided with the rise of <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> and led to a great deal of exposure for this brand of synthpop, creating what has been characterised as a <a href="/wiki/Second_British_Invasion" title="Second British Invasion">second British Invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-184">[184]</a></sup> Some more traditional rock bands adapted to the video age and profited from MTV's <a href="/wiki/Airplay" title="Airplay">airplay</a>, most obviously <a href="/wiki/Dire_Straits" title="Dire Straits">Dire Straits</a>, whose "<a href="/wiki/Money_for_Nothing_(song)" title="Money for Nothing (song)">Money for Nothing</a>" gently poked fun at the station, despite the fact that it had helped make them international stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> but in general, guitar-oriented rock was commercially eclipsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-punk">Post-punk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Post-punk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-punk" title="Post-punk">Post-punk</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gothic_rock" title="Gothic rock">Gothic rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_music" title="Industrial music">Industrial music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2005-11-21_U2_@_MSG_by_ZG.JPG"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the band U2 performing on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG/220px-2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG/330px-2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG/440px-2005-11-21_U2_%40_MSG_by_ZG.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2005-11-21_U2_@_MSG_by_ZG.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/U2" title="U2">U2</a> performing at Madison Square Garden in November 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>If hardcore most directly pursued the stripped down aesthetic of punk, and new wave came to represent its commercial wing, post-punk emerged in the later 1970s and early 1980s as its more artistic and challenging side. Major influences beside punk bands were <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground" title="The Velvet Underground">the Velvet Underground</a>, the Who, Frank Zappa and <a href="/wiki/Captain_Beefheart" title="Captain Beefheart">Captain Beefheart</a>, and the New York-based <a href="/wiki/No_wave" title="No wave">no wave</a> scene which placed an emphasis on performance, including bands such as <a href="/wiki/James_Chance_and_the_Contortions" title="James Chance and the Contortions">James Chance and the Contortions</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_(American_band)" title="DNA (American band)">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sonic_Youth" title="Sonic Youth">Sonic Youth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup> Early contributors to the genre included the US bands <a href="/wiki/Pere_Ubu" title="Pere Ubu">Pere Ubu</a>, Devo, <a href="/wiki/The_Residents" title="The Residents">the Residents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talking_Heads" title="Talking Heads">Talking Heads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first wave of British post-punk included <a href="/wiki/Gang_of_Four_(band)" title="Gang of Four (band)">Gang of Four</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_and_the_Banshees" title="Siouxsie and the Banshees">Siouxsie and the Banshees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joy_Division" title="Joy Division">Joy Division</a>, who placed less emphasis on art than their US counterparts and more on the dark emotional qualities of their music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup> Bands like Siouxsie and the Banshees, <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus_(band)" title="Bauhaus (band)">Bauhaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Cure" title="The Cure">the Cure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Sisters_of_Mercy" title="The Sisters of Mercy">the Sisters of Mercy</a>, moved increasingly in this direction to found Gothic rock, which had become the basis of a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-culture" title="Sub-culture">sub-culture</a> by the early 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Similar emotional territory was pursued by Australian acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Birthday_Party_(band)" title="The Birthday Party (band)">the Birthday Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Cave" title="Nick Cave">Nick Cave</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup> Members of Bauhaus and Joy Division explored new stylistic territory as <a href="/wiki/Love_and_Rockets_(band)" title="Love and Rockets (band)">Love and Rockets</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Order_(band)" title="New Order (band)">New Order</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup> Another early post-punk movement was the industrial music<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> developed by British bands <a href="/wiki/Throbbing_Gristle" title="Throbbing Gristle">Throbbing Gristle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cabaret_Voltaire_(band)" title="Cabaret Voltaire (band)">Cabaret Voltaire</a>, and New York-based <a href="/wiki/Suicide_(band)" title="Suicide (band)">Suicide</a>, using a variety of electronic and sampling techniques that emulated the sound of industrial production and which would develop into a variety of forms of <a href="/wiki/Post-industrial_music" title="Post-industrial music">post-industrial music</a> in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second generation of British post-punk bands that broke through in the early 1980s, including <a href="/wiki/The_Fall_(band)" title="The Fall (band)">the Fall</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Pop_Group" title="The Pop Group">the Pop Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Mekons" title="The Mekons">the Mekons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echo_and_the_Bunnymen" title="Echo and the Bunnymen">Echo and the Bunnymen</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teardrop_Explodes" title="Teardrop Explodes">Teardrop Explodes</a>, tended to move away from dark sonic landscapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002PostPunk_187-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002PostPunk-187">[187]</a></sup> Arguably the most successful band to emerge from post-punk was Ireland's <a href="/wiki/U2" title="U2">U2</a>, who incorporated elements of religious imagery together with political commentary into their often anthemic music, and by the late 1980s had become one of the biggest bands in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Although many post-punk bands continued to record and perform, it declined as a movement in the mid-1980s as acts disbanded or moved off to explore other musical areas, but it has continued to influence the development of rock music and has been seen as a major element in the creation of the alternative rock movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_waves_and_genres_in_heavy_metal">New waves and genres in heavy metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: New waves and genres in heavy metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal music</a></div>
