<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">New wave of British heavy metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">Glam metal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_metal" title="Extreme metal">Extreme metal</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section should include only a <strong>brief</strong> summary of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal music</a>.</b> See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">Wikipedia:Summary style</a> for information on how to properly incorporate it into this article's main text. <small><i>(August 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Although many established bands continued to perform and record, heavy metal suffered a hiatus in the face of the punk movement in the mid-1970s. Part of the reaction saw the popularity of bands like Motörhead, who had adopted a punk sensibility, and Judas Priest, who created a stripped down sound, largely removing the remaining elements of blues music, from their 1978 album <i><a href="/wiki/Stained_Class" title="Stained Class">Stained Class</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002JudasPriest_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002JudasPriest-193">[193]</a></sup> This change of direction was compared to punk and in the late 1970s became known as the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a> (NWOBHM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> During this era, almost all heavy metal performers were male, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/All-female_band" title="All-female band">all-female band</a> <a href="/wiki/Girlschool" title="Girlschool">Girlschool</a> from the UK. These bands were soon followed by acts including <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vardis" title="Vardis">Vardis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Head_(band)" title="Diamond Head (band)">Diamond Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxon_(band)" title="Saxon (band)">Saxon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Def_Leppard" title="Def Leppard">Def Leppard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venom_(band)" title="Venom (band)">Venom</a>, many of which began to enjoy considerable success in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser2003_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walser2003-195">[195]</a></sup> In the same period <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Van_Halen" title="Eddie Van Halen">Eddie Van Halen</a> established himself as a metal guitar virtuoso after his band's <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen_(album)" title="Van Halen (album)">self-titled 1978 album</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christie2003_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christie2003-196">[196]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Randy_Rhoads" title="Randy Rhoads">Randy Rhoads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yngwie_Malmsteen" title="Yngwie Malmsteen">Yngwie Malmsteen</a> also became established virtuosos, associated with what would be known as the <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_metal" title="Neoclassical metal">neoclassical metal</a> style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the band Iron Maiden on stage with guitars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1157" data-file-width="1437" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/220px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/330px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/440px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, one of the central bands in the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a>, performing in Barcelona in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Inspired by NWOBHM and Van Halen's success, a metal scene began to develop in Southern California from the late 1970s, based on the clubs of L.A.'s <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Strip" title="Sunset Strip">Sunset Strip</a> and including such bands as <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Riot" title="Quiet Riot">Quiet Riot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ratt" title="Ratt">Ratt</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6tley_Cr%C3%BCe" title="Mötley Crüe">Mötley Crüe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/W.A.S.P." title="W.A.S.P.">W.A.S.P.</a>, who, along with similarly styled acts such as New York's <a href="/wiki/Twisted_Sister" title="Twisted Sister">Twisted Sister</a> and Pennsylvania's <a href="/wiki/Poison_(American_band)" title="Poison (American band)">Poison</a>, incorporated the theatrics (and sometimes makeup) of <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam rock</a> acts like Alice Cooper and Kiss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christie2003_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-Christie2003-196">[196]</a></sup> The lyrics of these glam metal bands characteristically emphasized <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">hedonism</a> and wild behavior and musically were distinguished by rapid-fire <a href="/wiki/Shred_guitar" title="Shred guitar">shred guitar</a> solos, anthemic choruses, and a relatively melodic, pop-oriented approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christie2003_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-Christie2003-196">[196]</a></sup> The most commercially significant release of the era being <i><a href="/wiki/Slippery_When_Wet" title="Slippery When Wet">Slippery When Wet</a></i> (1986) by <a href="/wiki/Bon_Jovi" title="Bon Jovi">Bon Jovi</a> from <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, selling over 12 million copies in the US alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> The album has been credited with widening the audience for the subgenre, particularly by appealing to women as well as the traditional male dominated audience, and opening the door to MTV and commercial success for other bands at the end of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> By the mid-1980s bands were beginning to emerge from the L.A. scene that pursued a less glam image and a rawer sound, particularly <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N' Roses">Guns N' Roses</a>, breaking through with the chart-topping <i><a href="/wiki/Appetite_for_Destruction" title="Appetite for Destruction">Appetite for Destruction</a></i> (1987), and <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane's Addiction">Jane's Addiction</a>, who emerged with their major label debut <i><a href="/wiki/Nothing%27s_Shocking" title="Nothing's Shocking">Nothing's Shocking</a></i>, the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1980s metal fragmented into several subgenres, including <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">thrash metal</a>, which developed in the US from the style known as <a href="/wiki/Speed_metal" title="Speed metal">speed metal</a>, under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">hardcore punk</a>, with low-register guitar riffs typically overlaid by <a href="/wiki/Shred_guitar" title="Shred guitar">shredding</a> leads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002HeavyMetal_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002HeavyMetal-201">[201]</a></sup> Lyrics often expressed <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilistic</a> views or deal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">social issues</a> using visceral, gory language. It was popularised by the "Big Four of Thrash": <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">Metallica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">Anthrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">Megadeth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">Slayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser2003_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walser2003-195">[195]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">Death metal</a> developed out of thrash, particularly influenced by the bands Venom and Slayer. Florida's <a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">Death</a> and the Bay Area's <a href="/wiki/Possessed_(band)" title="Possessed (band)">Possessed</a> emphasized lyrical elements of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabolism" title="Diabolism">diabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Millenarianism" title="Millenarianism">millenarianism</a>, with vocals usually delivered as guttural "<a href="/wiki/Death_growl" title="Death growl">death growls</a>," high-pitched <a href="/wiki/Screaming_(music)" title="Screaming (music)">screaming</a>, complemented by downtuned, highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" title="Distortion (guitar)">distorted</a> guitars and extremely fast <a href="/wiki/Bass_drum" title="Bass drum">double bass</a> percussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">Black metal</a>, again influenced by Venom and pioneered by Denmark's <a href="/wiki/Mercyful_Fate" title="Mercyful Fate">Mercyful Fate</a>, Switzerland's <a href="/wiki/Hellhammer" title="Hellhammer">Hellhammer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Frost" title="Celtic Frost">Celtic Frost</a>, and Sweden's <a href="/wiki/Bathory_(band)" title="Bathory (band)">Bathory</a>, had many similarities in sound to death metal, but was often intentionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lo-fi" title="Lo-fi">lo-fi</a> in production and placed greater emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">satanic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagan</a> themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Bathory were particularly important in inspiring the further subgenres of <a href="/wiki/Viking_metal" title="Viking metal">Viking metal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">folk metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christie2003p270_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christie2003p270-205">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Power_metal" title="Power metal">Power metal</a> emerged in Europe in the late 1980s as a reaction to the harshness of death and black metal and was established by Germany's <a href="/wiki/Helloween" title="Helloween">Helloween</a>, who combined a melodic approach with thrash's speed and energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> England's <a href="/wiki/DragonForce" title="DragonForce">DragonForce</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> and Florida's <a href="/wiki/Iced_Earth" title="Iced Earth">Iced Earth</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> have a sound indebted to NWOBHM, while acts such as Florida's <a href="/wiki/Kamelot" title="Kamelot">Kamelot</a>, Finland's <a href="/wiki/Nightwish" title="Nightwish">Nightwish</a>, Italy's <a href="/wiki/Rhapsody_of_Fire" title="Rhapsody of Fire">Rhapsody of Fire</a>, and Russia's <a href="/wiki/Catharsis_(Russian_band)" title="Catharsis (Russian band)">Catharsis</a> feature a keyboard-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphonic_power_metal" title="Symphonic power metal">"symphonic" sound</a>, sometimes employing orchestras and opera singers. In contrast to other subgenres <a href="/wiki/Doom_metal" title="Doom metal">doom metal</a>, influenced by Gothic rock, slowed down the music, with bands like England's <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Altar" title="Pagan Altar">Pagan Altar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Witchfinder_General_(band)" title="Witchfinder General (band)">Witchfinder General</a> and the United States' <a href="/wiki/Pentagram_(band)" title="Pentagram (band)">Pentagram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Vitus_(band)" title="Saint Vitus (band)">Saint Vitus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trouble_(band)" title="Trouble (band)">Trouble</a>, emphasizing melody, down-tuned guitars, a 'thicker' or 'heavier' sound and a sepulchral mood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> American bands such as <a href="/wiki/Queensr%C3%BFche" title="Queensrÿche">Queensrÿche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dream_Theater" title="Dream Theater">Dream Theater</a> pioneered an often instrumentally challenging fusion of NWOBHM and progressive rock called <a href="/wiki/Progressive_metal" title="Progressive metal">progressive metal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Symphony_X" title="Symphony X">Symphony X</a> combining aspects of power metal and classical music with the style, while Sweden's <a href="/wiki/Opeth" title="Opeth">Opeth</a> developed a unique style indebted to both death metal and atmospheric 1970s prog rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stylusreview_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-stylusreview-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heartland_rock">Heartland rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Heartland rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38,_Bruce_Springsteen,_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Bruce Springsteen on stage with a guitar" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="506" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38%2C_Bruce_Springsteen%2C_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1988-0719-38,_Bruce_Springsteen,_Konzert_in_der_DDR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bruce_Springsteen" title="Bruce Springsteen">Bruce Springsteen</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Berlin" title="East Berlin">East Berlin</a> in 1988</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heartland_rock" title="Heartland rock">Heartland rock</a></div>
<p>American working-class oriented heartland rock, characterized by a straightforward musical style, and a concern with the lives of ordinary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue-collar" title="Blue-collar">blue-collar</a> American people, developed in the second half of the 1970s. The term heartland rock was first used to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Midwest" title="American Midwest">Midwestern</a> <a href="/wiki/Arena_rock" title="Arena rock">arena rock</a> groups like <a href="/wiki/Kansas_(band)" title="Kansas (band)">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/REO_Speedwagon" title="REO Speedwagon">REO Speedwagon</a> and Styx, but which came to be associated with a more socially concerned form of roots rock more directly influenced by folk, country and rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> It has been seen as an American Midwest and <a href="/wiki/Rust_Belt" title="Rust Belt">Rust Belt</a> counterpart to West Coast country rock and the Southern rock of the American South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Led by figures who had initially been identified with punk and New Wave, it was most strongly influenced by acts such as Bob Dylan, the Byrds, Creedence Clearwater Revival and <a href="/wiki/Van_Morrison" title="Van Morrison">Van Morrison</a>, and the basic rock of 1960s garage and the Rolling Stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicHeartland_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicHeartland-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Exemplified by the commercial success of singer songwriters <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Springsteen" title="Bruce Springsteen">Bruce Springsteen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Seger" title="Bob Seger">Bob Seger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Petty" title="Tom Petty">Tom Petty</a>, along with less widely known acts such as <a href="/wiki/Southside_Johnny_and_the_Asbury_Jukes" title="Southside Johnny and the Asbury Jukes">Southside Johnny and the Asbury Jukes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Grushecky" title="Joe Grushecky">Joe Grushecky and the Houserockers</a>, it was partly a reaction to post-industrial urban decline in the East and Mid-West, often dwelling on issues of social disintegration and isolation, beside a form of good-time rock and roll revivalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicHeartland_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicHeartland-215">[215]</a></sup> The genre reached its commercial, artistic and influential peak in the mid-1980s, with Springsteen's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Born_in_the_USA" title="Born in the USA">Born in the USA</a></i> (1984), topping the charts worldwide and spawning a series of top ten singles, together with the arrival of artists including <a href="/wiki/John_Mellencamp" title="John Mellencamp">John Mellencamp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Earle" title="Steve Earle">Steve Earle</a> and more gentle singer/songwriters such as <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Hornsby" title="Bruce Hornsby">Bruce Hornsby</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicHeartland_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicHeartland-215">[215]</a></sup> It can also be heard as an influence on artists as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Billy_Joel" title="Billy Joel">Billy Joel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kid_Rock" title="Kid Rock">Kid Rock</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Killers" title="The Killers">the Killers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heartland rock faded away as a recognized genre by the early 1990s, as rock music in general, and blue collar and white working class themes in particular, lost influence with younger audiences, and as heartland's artists turned to more personal works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicHeartland_215-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicHeartland-215">[215]</a></sup> Many heartland rock artists continue to record today with critical and commercial success, most notably Bruce Springsteen, Tom Petty and John Mellencamp, although their works have become more personal and experimental and no longer fit easily into a single genre. Newer artists whose music would perhaps have been labelled heartland rock had it been released in the 1970s or 1980s, such as Missouri's <a href="/wiki/The_Bottle_Rockets" title="The Bottle Rockets">Bottle Rockets</a> and Illinois' <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tupelo" title="Uncle Tupelo">Uncle Tupelo</a>, often find themselves labeled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alt-country" title="Alt-country">alt-country</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_alternative_rock">Emergence of alternative rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Emergence of alternative rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">Alternative rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jangle_pop" title="Jangle pop">Jangle pop</a>, <a href="/wiki/College_rock" title="College rock">college rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indie_pop" title="Indie pop">indie pop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dream_pop" title="Dream pop">dream pop</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shoegazing" title="Shoegazing">shoegazing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of the band R.E.M. on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg/220px-Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg/330px-Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg/440px-Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Padova_REM_concert_July_22_2003_blue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/R.E.M." title="R.E.M.">R.E.M.</a> was a successful <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> band in the 1980s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term alternative rock was coined in the early 1980s to describe rock artists who did not fit into the mainstream genres of the time. Bands dubbed "alternative" had no unified style, but were all seen as distinct from mainstream music. Alternative bands were linked by their collective debt to punk rock, through hardcore, New Wave or the post-punk movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> Important alternative rock bands of the 1980s in the US included <a href="/wiki/R.E.M." title="R.E.M.">R.E.M.</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCsker_D%C3%BC" title="Hüsker Dü">Hüsker Dü</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane's Addiction">Jane's Addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonic_Youth" title="Sonic Youth">Sonic Youth</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pixies" title="Pixies">Pixies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> and in the UK <a href="/wiki/The_Cure" title="The Cure">the Cure</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Order_(band)" title="New Order (band)">New Order</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Jesus_and_Mary_Chain" title="The Jesus and Mary Chain">the Jesus and Mary Chain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Smiths" title="The Smiths">the Smiths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup> Artists were largely confined to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_record_labels" title="Independent record labels">independent record labels</a>, building an extensive underground music scene based on <a href="/wiki/Campus_radio" title="Campus radio">college radio</a>, fanzines, touring, and word-of-mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> They rejected the dominant synthpop of the early 1980s, marking a return to group-based guitar rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMCollegeRock_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMCollegeRock-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Few of these early bands achieved mainstream success, although exceptions to this rule include R.E.M., the Smiths, and the Cure. Despite a general lack of spectacular album sales, the original alternative rock bands exerted a considerable influence on the generation of musicians who came of age in the 1980s and ended up breaking through to mainstream success in the 1990s. Styles of alternative rock in the U.S. during the 1980s included <a href="/wiki/Jangle_pop" title="Jangle pop">jangle pop</a>, associated with the early recordings of R.E.M., which incorporated the ringing guitars of mid-1960s pop and rock, and college rock, used to describe alternative bands that began in the college circuit and college radio, including acts such as <a href="/wiki/10,000_Maniacs" title="10,000 Maniacs">10,000 Maniacs</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Feelies" title="The Feelies">the Feelies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> In the UK Gothic rock was dominant in the early 1980s, but by the end of the decade indie or dream pop<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> like <a href="/wiki/Primal_Scream" title="Primal Scream">Primal Scream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogshed" title="Bogshed">Bogshed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Half_Man_Half_Biscuit" title="Half Man Half Biscuit">Half Man Half Biscuit</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Wedding_Present" title="The Wedding Present">the Wedding Present</a>, and what were dubbed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shoegaze" title="Shoegaze">shoegaze</a> bands like <a href="/wiki/My_Bloody_Valentine_(band)" title="My Bloody Valentine (band)">My Bloody Valentine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ride_(band)" title="Ride (band)">Ride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lush_(band)" title="Lush (band)">Lush</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chapterhouse_(band)" title="Chapterhouse (band)">Chapterhouse</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boo_Radleys" title="Boo Radleys">Boo Radleys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Particularly vibrant was the <a href="/wiki/Madchester" title="Madchester">Madchester</a> scene, produced such bands as <a href="/wiki/Happy_Mondays" title="Happy Mondays">Happy Mondays</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Inspiral_Carpets" title="Inspiral Carpets">Inspiral Carpets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Roses" title="The Stone Roses">Stone Roses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> The next decade would see the success of <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">grunge</a> in the United States and <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> in the United Kingdom, bringing alternative rock into the mainstream.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative">Alternative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Alternative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grunge">Grunge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Grunge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">Grunge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nirvana_around_1992.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of two members of the band Nirvana on stage with guitars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="367" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Nirvana_around_1992.jpg/220px-Nirvana_around_1992.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Nirvana_around_1992.jpg/330px-Nirvana_around_1992.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Nirvana_around_1992.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nirvana_around_1992.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)" title="Nirvana (band)">Nirvana</a> in 1992</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Disaffected by commercialized and highly produced pop and rock in the mid-1980s, bands in <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington state</a> (particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> area) formed a new style of rock which sharply contrasted with the mainstream music of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic_grunge_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_grunge-229">[229]</a></sup> The developing genre came to be known as "grunge", a term descriptive of the dirty sound of the music and the unkempt appearance of most musicians, who actively rebelled against the over-groomed images of other artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic_grunge_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_grunge-229">[229]</a></sup> Grunge fused elements of <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">hardcore punk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a> into a single sound, and made heavy use of guitar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" title="Distortion (guitar)">distortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">fuzz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Audio_feedback" title="Audio feedback">feedback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic_grunge_229-2"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_grunge-229">[229]</a></sup> The lyrics were typically apathetic and angst-filled, and often concerned themes such as social alienation and entrapment, although it was also known for its dark humor and parodies of commercial rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic_grunge_229-3"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_grunge-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bands such as <a href="/wiki/Green_River_(band)" title="Green River (band)">Green River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soundgarden" title="Soundgarden">Soundgarden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Melvins" title="Melvins">Melvins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skin_Yard" title="Skin Yard">Skin Yard</a> pioneered the genre, with <a href="/wiki/Mudhoney" title="Mudhoney">Mudhoney</a> becoming the most successful by the end of the decade. Grunge remained largely a local phenomenon until 1991, when <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)" title="Nirvana (band)">Nirvana</a>'s album <i><a href="/wiki/Nevermind" title="Nevermind">Nevermind</a></i> became a huge success because of its song "<a href="/wiki/Smells_Like_Teen_Spirit" title="Smells Like Teen Spirit">Smells Like Teen Spirit</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> <i>Nevermind</i> was more melodic than its predecessors, by signing to Geffen Records the band was one of the first to employ traditional corporate promotion and marketing mechanisms such as an MTV video, in store displays and the use of radio "consultants" who promoted airplay at major mainstream rock stations. During 1991 and 1992, other grunge albums such as <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Jam" title="Pearl Jam">Pearl Jam</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ten_(Pearl_Jam_album)" title="Ten (Pearl Jam album)">Ten</a></i>, Soundgarden's <i><a href="/wiki/Badmotorfinger" title="Badmotorfinger">Badmotorfinger</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Chains" title="Alice in Chains">Alice in Chains</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Dirt_(Alice_in_Chains_album)" title="Dirt (Alice in Chains album)">Dirt</a></i>, along with the <i><a href="/wiki/Temple_of_the_Dog" title="Temple of the Dog">Temple of the Dog</a></i> album featuring members of Pearl Jam and Soundgarden, became among the 100 top-selling albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> Major record labels signed most of the remaining grunge bands in Seattle, while a second influx of acts moved to the city in the hope of success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Azerrad419_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Azerrad419-232">[232]</a></sup> However, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_Kurt_Cobain" title="Death of Kurt Cobain">death of Kurt Cobain</a> and the subsequent break-up of Nirvana in 1994, touring problems for Pearl Jam and the departure of Alice in Chains' lead singer <a href="/wiki/Layne_Staley" title="Layne Staley">Layne Staley</a> in 1996, the genre began to decline, partly to be overshadowed by Britpop and more commercial sounding <a href="/wiki/Post-grunge" title="Post-grunge">post-grunge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPostgrunge_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPostgrunge-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britpop">Britpop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Britpop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of Noel and Liam Gallagher of the band Oasis on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="449" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg/220px-Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg/330px-Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg/440px-Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oasis_Liam_and_Noel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a> performing in 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Britpop emerged from the British alternative rock scene of the early 1990s and was characterised by bands particularly influenced by British guitar music of the 1960s and 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Smiths" title="The Smiths">The Smiths</a> were a major influence, as were bands of the <a href="/wiki/Madchester" title="Madchester">Madchester</a> scene, which had dissolved in the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusicBritpop_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-allmusicBritpop-73">[73]</a></sup> The movement has been seen partly as a reaction against various U.S. based, musical and cultural trends in the late 1980s and early 1990s, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">grunge</a> phenomenon and as a reassertion of a British rock identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup> Britpop was varied in style, but often used catchy tunes and hooks, beside lyrics with particularly British concerns and the adoption of the iconography of the 1960s British Invasion, including the symbols of British identity previously utilised by the mods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> It was launched around 1992 with releases by groups such as <a href="/wiki/Suede_(band)" title="Suede (band)">Suede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a>, who were soon joined by others including <a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(band)" title="Pulp (band)">Pulp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supergrass" title="Supergrass">Supergrass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elastica" title="Elastica">Elastica</a>, who produced a series of top ten albums and singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup> For a while the contest between Blur and Oasis was built by the popular press into "The Battle of Britpop", initially won by Blur, but with Oasis achieving greater long-term and international success, directly influencing a third generation of Britpop bands, including <a href="/wiki/The_Boo_Radleys" title="The Boo Radleys">The Boo Radleys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Colour_Scene" title="Ocean Colour Scene">Ocean Colour Scene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cast_(band)" title="Cast (band)">Cast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Britpop groups brought British alternative rock into the mainstream and formed the backbone of a larger British cultural movement known as <a href="/wiki/Cool_Britannia" title="Cool Britannia">Cool Britannia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Although its more popular bands, particularly Blur and Oasis, were able to spread their commercial success overseas, especially to the United States, the movement had largely fallen apart by the end of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-grunge">Post-grunge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Post-grunge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-grunge" title="Post-grunge">Post-grunge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the Foo Fighters on stage with instruments" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1836" data-file-width="2760" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg/220px-Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg/330px-Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg/440px-Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foo_Fighters_Live_29.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Foo_Fighters" title="Foo Fighters">Foo Fighters</a> performing an acoustic show in 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term post-grunge was coined for the generation of bands that followed the emergence into the mainstream and subsequent hiatus of the Seattle grunge bands. Post-grunge bands emulated their attitudes and music, but with a more radio-friendly commercially oriented sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPostgrunge_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPostgrunge-233">[233]</a></sup> Often they worked through the major labels and came to incorporate diverse influences from jangle pop, pop-punk, <a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">alternative metal</a> or hard rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPostgrunge_233-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPostgrunge-233">[233]</a></sup> The term post-grunge originally was meant to be pejorative, suggesting that they were simply musically derivative, or a cynical response to an "authentic" rock movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-237">[237]</a></sup> Originally, grunge bands that emerged when grunge was mainstream and were suspected of emulating the grunge sound were pejoratively labelled as post-grunge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-237">[237]</a></sup> From 1994, former Nirvana drummer <a href="/wiki/Dave_Grohl" title="Dave Grohl">Dave Grohl</a>'s new band, the <a href="/wiki/Foo_Fighters" title="Foo Fighters">Foo Fighters</a>, helped popularize the genre and define its parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FooFighters_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FooFighters-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although male bands predominated post-grunge, female solo artist <a href="/wiki/Alanis_Morissette" title="Alanis Morissette">Alanis Morissette</a>'s 1995 album <i><a href="/wiki/Jagged_Little_Pill" title="Jagged Little Pill">Jagged Little Pill</a></i>, labelled as post-grunge, also became a multi-platinum hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Alanis_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Alanis-239">[239]</a></sup> Post-grunge morphed during the late 1990s as post-grunge bands like <a href="/wiki/Creed_(band)" title="Creed (band)">Creed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickelback" title="Nickelback">Nickelback</a> emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_237-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-237">[237]</a></sup> Bands like Creed and Nickelback took post-grunge into the 21st century with considerable commercial success, abandoning most of the angst and anger of the original movement for more conventional anthems, narratives and romantic songs, and were followed in this vein by newer acts including <a href="/wiki/Shinedown" title="Shinedown">Shinedown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seether" title="Seether">Seether</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Doors_Down" title="3 Doors Down">3 Doors Down</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puddle_of_Mudd" title="Puddle of Mudd">Puddle of Mudd</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_237-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pop_punk">Pop punk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Pop punk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pop_punk" title="Pop punk">Pop punk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the group Green Day on stage with instruments" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG/220px-Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG/330px-Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG/440px-Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Green_day_Live_5_june_2013_in_Rome5.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Green_Day" title="Green Day">Green Day</a> performing in 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The origins of 1990s pop punk can be seen in the more song-oriented bands of the 1970s punk movement like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Buzzcocks" title="The Buzzcocks">the Buzzcocks</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">the Clash</a>, commercially successful New Wave acts such as <a href="/wiki/The_Jam" title="The Jam">the Jam</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Undertones" title="The Undertones">the Undertones</a>, and the more hardcore-influenced elements of alternative rock in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LambPunkPop_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-LambPunkPop-240">[240]</a></sup> Pop-punk tends to use power-pop melodies and chord changes with speedy punk tempos and loud guitars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> Punk music provided the inspiration for some California-based bands on independent labels in the early 1990s, including <a href="/wiki/Rancid_(band)" title="Rancid (band)">Rancid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennywise_(band)" title="Pennywise (band)">Pennywise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weezer" title="Weezer">Weezer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_Day" title="Green Day">Green Day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LambPunkPop_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-LambPunkPop-240">[240]</a></sup> In 1994 Green Day moved to a major label and produced the album <i><a href="/wiki/Dookie" title="Dookie">Dookie</a></i>, which found a new, largely teenage, audience and proved a surprise diamond-selling success, leading to a series of hit singles, including two number ones in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> They were soon followed by the <a href="/wiki/Weezer_(1994_album)" title="Weezer (1994 album)">eponymous début from Weezer</a>, which spawned three top ten singles in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> This success opened the door for the multi-platinum sales of metallic punk band <a href="/wiki/The_Offspring" title="The Offspring">the Offspring</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Smash_(The_Offspring_album)" title="Smash (The Offspring album)">Smash</a></i> (1994).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> This first wave of pop punk reached its commercial peak with Green Day's <i><a href="/wiki/Nimrod_(album)" title="Nimrod (album)">Nimrod</a></i> (1997) and The Offspring's <i><a href="/wiki/Americana_(The_Offspring_album)" title="Americana (The Offspring album)">Americana</a></i> (1998).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002GreenDay.26Offspring_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002GreenDay.26Offspring-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>A second wave of pop punk was spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Blink-182" title="Blink-182">Blink-182</a>, with their breakthrough album <i><a href="/wiki/Enema_of_the_State" title="Enema of the State">Enema of the State</a></i> (1999), followed by bands such as <a href="/wiki/Good_Charlotte" title="Good Charlotte">Good Charlotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simple_Plan" title="Simple Plan">Simple Plan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sum_41" title="Sum 41">Sum 41</a>, who made use of humour in their videos and had a more radio-friendly tone to their music, while retaining the speed, some of the attitude and even the look of 1970s punk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LambPunkPop_240-2"><a href="#cite_note-LambPunkPop-240">[240]</a></sup> Later pop-punk bands, including <a href="/wiki/All_Time_Low" title="All Time Low">All Time Low</a>,<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5_Seconds_Of_Summer" title="5 Seconds Of Summer">5 Seconds Of Summer</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_All-American_Rejects" title="The All-American Rejects">the All-American Rejects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fall_Out_Boy" title="Fall Out Boy">Fall Out Boy</a>, had a sound that has been described as closer to 1980s hardcore, while still achieving commercial success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LambPunkPop_240-3"><a href="#cite_note-LambPunkPop-240">[240]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indie_rock">Indie rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Indie rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indie_rock" title="Indie rock">Indie rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riot_Grrrl" title="Riot Grrrl">Riot Grrrl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lo-fi_music" title="Lo-fi music">Lo-fi music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_rock" title="Post rock">Post rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Math_rock" title="Math rock">Math rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_rock" title="Space rock">Space rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sadcore" title="Sadcore">Sadcore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_pop" title="Baroque pop">Baroque pop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pavement,_the_band,_in_Tokyo.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of five members of the group Pavement standing in front of a brick wall" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1000" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg/220px-Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg/330px-Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg/440px-Pavement%2C_the_band%2C_in_Tokyo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pavement,_the_band,_in_Tokyo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lo-fi indie rock band <a href="/wiki/Pavement_(band)" title="Pavement (band)">Pavement</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1980s the terms indie rock and alternative rock were used interchangeably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicIndie_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicIndie-244">[244]</a></sup> By the mid-1990s, as elements of the movement began to attract mainstream interest, particularly grunge and then Britpop, post-grunge and pop-punk, the term alternative began to lose its meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicIndie_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicIndie-244">[244]</a></sup> Those bands following the less commercial contours of the scene were increasingly referred to by the label indie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicIndie_244-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicIndie-244">[244]</a></sup> They characteristically attempted to retain control of their careers by releasing albums on their own or small independent labels, while relying on touring, word-of-mouth, and airplay on independent or college radio stations for promotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicIndie_244-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicIndie-244">[244]</a></sup> Linked by an ethos more than a musical approach, the indie rock movement encompassed a wide range of styles, from hard-edged, grunge-influenced bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Cranberries" title="The Cranberries">the Cranberries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Superchunk" title="Superchunk">Superchunk</a>, through do-it-yourself experimental bands like <a href="/wiki/Pavement_(band)" title="Pavement (band)">Pavement</a>, to punk-folk singers such as <a href="/wiki/Ani_DiFranco" title="Ani DiFranco">Ani DiFranco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative_221-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002UKAlternative-221">[221]</a></sup> It has been noted that indie rock has a relatively high proportion of female artists compared with preceding rock genres, a tendency exemplified by the development of feminist-informed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riot_Grrrl" title="Riot Grrrl">Riot Grrrl</a> music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> Many countries have developed an extensive local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indie_(music)" title="Indie (music)">indie</a> scene, flourishing with bands with enough popularity to survive inside the respective country, but virtually unknown outside them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the 1990s many recognisable subgenres, most with their origins in the late 1980s alternative movement, were included under the umbrella of indie. Lo-fi eschewed polished recording techniques for a D.I.Y. ethos and was spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Beck" title="Beck">Beck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sebadoh" title="Sebadoh">Sebadoh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pavement_(band)" title="Pavement (band)">Pavement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002USAlternative_220-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002USAlternative-220">[220]</a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/Talk_Talk" title="Talk Talk">Talk Talk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slint" title="Slint">Slint</a> helped inspire both post rock, an experimental style influenced by <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a>, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Bark_Psychosis" title="Bark Psychosis">Bark Psychosis</a> and taken up by acts such as <a href="/wiki/Tortoise_(band)" title="Tortoise (band)">Tortoise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stereolab" title="Stereolab">Stereolab</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laika_(band)" title="Laika (band)">Laika</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._Taylor.2C_2006_pp._154-5_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-S._Taylor.2C_2006_pp._154-5-247">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMpostrock_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMpostrock-248">[248]</a></sup> as well as leading to more dense and complex, guitar-based math rock, developed by acts like <a href="/wiki/Polvo" title="Polvo">Polvo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chavez_(band)" title="Chavez (band)">Chavez</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Space rock looked back to progressive roots, with drone heavy and minimalist acts like <a href="/wiki/Spacemen_3" title="Spacemen 3">Spacemen 3</a>, the two bands created out of its split, <a href="/wiki/Spectrum_(band)" title="Spectrum (band)">Spectrum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spiritualized" title="Spiritualized">Spiritualized</a>, and later groups including <a href="/wiki/Flying_Saucer_Attack" title="Flying Saucer Attack">Flying Saucer Attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Godspeed_You!_Black_Emperor" title="Godspeed You! Black Emperor">Godspeed You! Black Emperor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quickspace" title="Quickspace">Quickspace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Sadcore" title="Sadcore">Sadcore</a> emphasised pain and suffering through melodic use of acoustic and electronic instrumentation in the music of bands like <a href="/wiki/American_Music_Club" title="American Music Club">American Music Club</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_House_Painters" title="Red House Painters">Red House Painters</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> while the revival of Baroque pop reacted against lo-fi and experimental music by placing an emphasis on melody and classical instrumentation, with artists like <a href="/wiki/Arcade_Fire" title="Arcade Fire">Arcade Fire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belle_and_Sebastian" title="Belle and Sebastian">Belle and Sebastian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Wainwright" title="Rufus Wainwright">Rufus Wainwright</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_metal.2C_rap_rock_and_nu_metal">Alternative metal, rap rock and nu metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Alternative metal, rap rock and nu metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal music</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_American_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of American Heavy Metal">New Wave of American Heavy Metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">Alternative metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rap_rock" title="Rap rock">Rap rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rap_metal" title="Rap metal">Rap metal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">Nu metal</a></div>
<p>Alternative metal emerged from the hardcore scene of alternative rock in the US in the later 1980s, but gained a wider audience after grunge broke into the mainstream in the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicAltMetal_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicAltMetal-253">[253]</a></sup> Early alternative metal bands mixed a wide variety of genres with hardcore and heavy metal sensibilities, with acts like <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane's Addiction">Jane's Addiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primus_(band)" title="Primus (band)">Primus</a> utilizing progressive rock, <a href="/wiki/Soundgarden" title="Soundgarden">Soundgarden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corrosion_of_Conformity" title="Corrosion of Conformity">Corrosion of Conformity</a> using garage punk, <a href="/wiki/The_Jesus_Lizard" title="The Jesus Lizard">the Jesus Lizard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helmet_(band)" title="Helmet (band)">Helmet</a> mixing <a href="/wiki/Noise_rock" title="Noise rock">noise rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ministry_(band)" title="Ministry (band)">Ministry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nine_Inch_Nails" title="Nine Inch Nails">Nine Inch Nails</a> influenced by <a href="/wiki/Industrial_music" title="Industrial music">industrial music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monster_Magnet" title="Monster Magnet">Monster Magnet</a> moving into <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_music" title="Psychedelic music">psychedelia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantera" title="Pantera">Pantera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepultura" title="Sepultura">Sepultura</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_Zombie_(band)" title="White Zombie (band)">White Zombie</a> creating <a href="/wiki/Groove_metal" title="Groove metal">groove metal</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Biohazard_(band)" title="Biohazard (band)">Biohazard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faith_No_More" title="Faith No More">Faith No More</a> turned to <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a> and rap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicAltMetal_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicAltMetal-253">[253]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linkin_Park_@_Sonisphere.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the group Linkin Park performing on and outdoor stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1441" data-file-width="3204" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg/275px-Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg/413px-Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg/550px-Linkin_Park_%40_Sonisphere.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linkin_Park_@_Sonisphere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Linkin_Park" title="Linkin Park">Linkin Park</a> performing in 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hip hop had gained attention from rock acts in the early 1980s, including The Clash with "<a href="/wiki/The_Magnificent_Seven_(song)" title="The Magnificent Seven (song)">The Magnificent Seven</a>" (1980) and Blondie with "<a href="/wiki/Rapture_(Blondie_song)" title="Rapture (Blondie song)">Rapture</a>" (1980).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> Early crossover acts included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Run_DMC" title="Run DMC">Run DMC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Beastie_Boys" title="Beastie Boys">Beastie Boys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanneh_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanneh-256">[256]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> rapper <a href="/wiki/Esham" title="Esham">Esham</a> became known for his "acid rap" style, which fused rapping with a sound that was often based in rock and heavy metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keyes_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keyes-257">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> Rappers who sampled rock songs included <a href="/wiki/Ice-T" title="Ice-T">Ice-T</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Fat_Boys" title="The Fat Boys">The Fat Boys</a>, <a href="/wiki/LL_Cool_J" title="LL Cool J">LL Cool J</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_Enemy_(band)" title="Public Enemy (band)">Public Enemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whodini" title="Whodini">Whodini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMRap-metal_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMRap-metal-259">[259]</a></sup> The mixing of thrash metal and rap was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">Anthrax</a> on their 1987 comedy-influenced single "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_the_Man_(EP)" title="I'm the Man (EP)">I'm the Man</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMRap-metal_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMRap-metal-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1990, <a href="/wiki/Faith_No_More" title="Faith No More">Faith No More</a> broke into the mainstream with their single "<a href="/wiki/Epic_(Faith_No_More_song)" title="Epic (Faith No More song)">Epic</a>", often seen as the first truly successful combination of heavy metal with rap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002FaithNoMore_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002FaithNoMore-260">[260]</a></sup> This paved the way for the success of existing bands like <a href="/wiki/24-7_Spyz" title="24-7 Spyz">24-7 Spyz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Living_Colour" title="Living Colour">Living Colour</a>, and new acts including <a href="/wiki/Rage_Against_the_Machine" title="Rage Against the Machine">Rage Against the Machine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_Hot_Chili_Peppers" title="Red Hot Chili Peppers">Red Hot Chili Peppers</a>, who all fused rock and hip hop among other influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMRap-metal_259-2"><a href="#cite_note-AMRap-metal-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GriersonRapRock_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-GriersonRapRock-261">[261]</a></sup> Among the first wave of performers to gain mainstream success as rap rock were <a href="/wiki/311_(band)" title="311 (band)">311</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bloodhound_Gang" title="Bloodhound Gang">Bloodhound Gang</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kid_Rock" title="Kid Rock">Kid Rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> A more metallic sound - <i>nu metal</i> - was pursued by bands including <a href="/wiki/Limp_Bizkit" title="Limp Bizkit">Limp Bizkit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">Korn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">Slipknot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMRap-metal_259-3"><a href="#cite_note-AMRap-metal-259">[259]</a></sup> Later in the decade this style, which contained a mix of grunge, punk, metal, rap and turntable <a href="/wiki/Scratching" title="Scratching">scratching</a>, spawned a wave of successful bands like <a href="/wiki/Linkin_Park" title="Linkin Park">Linkin Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/P.O.D." title="P.O.D.">P.O.D.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Staind" title="Staind">Staind</a>, who were often classified as rap metal or nu metal, the first of which are the best-selling band of the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, nu metal reached its peak with albums like Staind's <i><a href="/wiki/Break_the_Cycle" title="Break the Cycle">Break the Cycle</a></i>, P.O.D's <i><a href="/wiki/Satellite_(P.O.D._album)" title="Satellite (P.O.D. album)">Satellite</a></i>, Slipknot's <i><a href="/wiki/Iowa_(album)" title="Iowa (album)">Iowa</a></i> and Linkin Park's <i><a href="/wiki/Hybrid_Theory" title="Hybrid Theory">Hybrid Theory</a></i>. New bands also emerged like <a href="/wiki/Disturbed_(band)" title="Disturbed (band)">Disturbed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Godsmack" title="Godsmack">Godsmack</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papa_Roach" title="Papa Roach">Papa Roach</a>, whose major label début <i><a href="/wiki/Infest_(album)" title="Infest (album)">Infest</a></i> became a platinum hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Korn's long-awaited fifth album <i><a href="/wiki/Untouchables_(album)" title="Untouchables (album)">Untouchables</a></i>, and Papa Roach's second album <i><a href="/wiki/Lovehatetragedy" title="Lovehatetragedy">Lovehatetragedy</a></i>, did not sell as well as their previous releases, while nu metal bands were played more infrequently on rock radio stations and <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> began focusing on <a href="/wiki/Pop_punk" title="Pop punk">pop punk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">emo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAngeloMTV_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAngeloMTV-267">[267]</a></sup> Since then, many bands have changed to a more conventional hard rock, heavy metal, or electronic music sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAngeloMTV_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-DAngeloMTV-267">[267]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Britpop">Post-Britpop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Post-Britpop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-Britpop" title="Post-Britpop">Post-Britpop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coldplay-3.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of four members of Coldplay performing with instruments in front of a brick building" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1562" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Coldplay-3.jpg/275px-Coldplay-3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Coldplay-3.jpg/413px-Coldplay-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Coldplay-3.jpg/550px-Coldplay-3.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coldplay-3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Coldplay" title="Coldplay">Coldplay</a> in 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From about 1997, as dissatisfaction grew with the concept of Cool Britannia, and Britpop as a movement began to dissolve, emerging bands began to avoid the Britpop label while still producing music derived from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris2004_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris2004-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> Many of these bands tended to mix elements of British traditional rock (or British trad rock),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> particularly the Beatles, Rolling Stones and <a href="/wiki/Small_Faces" title="Small Faces">Small Faces</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patridis2004_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patridis2004-271">[271]</a></sup> with American influences, including post-grunge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicTravis_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicTravis-273">[273]</a></sup> Drawn from across the United Kingdom (with several important bands emerging from the north of England, Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland), the themes of their music tended to be less parochially centered on British, English and London life and more introspective than had been the case with Britpop at its height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> This, beside a greater willingness to engage with the American press and fans, may have helped some of them in achieving international success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowling2005_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dowling2005-276">[276]</a></sup></p>
<p>Post Britpop bands have been seen as presenting the image of the rock star as an ordinary person and their increasingly melodic music was criticised for being bland or derivative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> Post Britpop bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Verve" title="The Verve">the Verve</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Urban_Hymns" title="Urban Hymns">Urban Hymns</a></i> (1997), <a href="/wiki/Radiohead" title="Radiohead">Radiohead</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/OK_Computer" title="OK Computer">OK Computer</a></i> (1997), <a href="/wiki/Travis_(band)" title="Travis (band)">Travis</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who" title="The Man Who">The Man Who</a></i> (1999), <a href="/wiki/Stereophonics" title="Stereophonics">Stereophonics</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Performance_and_Cocktails" title="Performance and Cocktails">Performance and Cocktails</a></i> (1999), <a href="/wiki/Feeder" title="Feeder">Feeder</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Echo_Park_(album)" title="Echo Park (album)">Echo Park</a></i> (2001) and particularly <a href="/wiki/Coldplay" title="Coldplay">Coldplay</a> from their debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Parachutes_(album)" title="Parachutes (album)">Parachutes</a></i> (2000), achieved much wider international success than most of the Britpop groups that had preceded them, and were some of the most commercially successful acts of the late 1990s and early 2000s, arguably providing a launchpad for the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">garage rock</a> or <a href="/wiki/Post-punk_revival" title="Post-punk revival">post-punk revival</a>, which has also been seen as a reaction to their introspective brand of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicTravis_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicTravis-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicStereophonics_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicStereophonics-279">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicColdplay_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicColdplay-280">[280]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s.E2.80.93present">2000s–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: 2000s–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
