</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-hardcore_and_emo">Post-hardcore and emo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Post-hardcore and emo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Post-hardcore" title="Post-hardcore">Post-hardcore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">Emo</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Screamo" title="Screamo">Screamo</a></div>
<p>Post-hardcore developed in the US, particularly in the Chicago and Washington, D.C areas, in the early to mid-1980s, with bands that were inspired by the do-it-yourself ethics and guitar-heavy music of hardcore punk, but influenced by post-punk, adopting longer song formats, more complex musical structures and sometimes more melodic vocal styles. Existing bands that moved on from hardcore included <a href="/wiki/Fugazi" title="Fugazi">Fugazi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMPost-Hardcore_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMPost-Hardcore-281">[281]</a></sup> From the late 1980s they were followed by bands including <a href="/wiki/Quicksand_(American_band)" title="Quicksand (American band)">Quicksand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMQuicksand_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMQuicksand-282">[282]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Girls_Against_Boys" title="Girls Against Boys">Girls Against Boys</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Jesus_Lizard" title="The Jesus Lizard">The Jesus Lizard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> Bands that formed in the 1990s included <a href="/wiki/Thursday_(band)" title="Thursday (band)">Thursday</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMThursday_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMThursday-285">[285]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thrice" title="Thrice">Thrice</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMThrice_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMThrice-286">[286]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finch_(U.S._band)" title="Finch (U.S. band)">Finch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Poison_the_Well_(band)" title="Poison the Well (band)">Poison the Well</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fugazi.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of members of the band Fugazi performing on state with the heads of an audience in the foreground" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1152" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fugazi.jpg/220px-Fugazi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fugazi.jpg/330px-Fugazi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fugazi.jpg/440px-Fugazi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fugazi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Members of <a href="/wiki/Fugazi" title="Fugazi">Fugazi</a> performing in 2002</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Emo also emerged from the hardcore scene in 1980s Washington, D.C., initially as "emocore", used as a term to describe bands who favored expressive vocals over the more common abrasive, barking style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup> The style was pioneered by bands <a href="/wiki/Rites_of_Spring" title="Rites of Spring">Rites of Spring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embrace_(American_band)" title="Embrace (American band)">Embrace</a>, the last formed by <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacKaye" title="Ian MacKaye">Ian MacKaye</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Dischord_Records" title="Dischord Records">Dischord Records</a> became a major centre for the emerging D.C. emo scene, releasing work by Rites of Spring, <a href="/wiki/Dag_Nasty" title="Dag Nasty">Dag Nasty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation_of_Ulysses" title="Nation of Ulysses">Nation of Ulysses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fugazi" title="Fugazi">Fugazi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup> Fugazi emerged as the definitive early emo band, gaining a fanbase among alternative rock followers, not least for their overtly anti-commercial stance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup> The early emo scene operated as an underground, with short-lived bands releasing small-run vinyl records on tiny independent labels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup> The mid-1990s sound of emo was defined by bands like <a href="/wiki/Jawbreaker_(band)" title="Jawbreaker (band)">Jawbreaker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunny_Day_Real_Estate" title="Sunny Day Real Estate">Sunny Day Real Estate</a> who incorporated elements of grunge and more melodic rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup> Only after the breakthrough of grunge and pop punk into the mainstream did emo come to wider attention with the success of Weezer's <i><a href="/wiki/Pinkerton_(album)" title="Pinkerton (album)">Pinkerton</a></i> (1996) album, which utilised pop punk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-4"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup> Late 1990s bands drew on the work of Fugazi, SDRE, Jawbreaker and Weezer, including <a href="/wiki/The_Promise_Ring" title="The Promise Ring">The Promise Ring</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Get_Up_Kids" title="The Get Up Kids">The Get Up Kids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Braid_(band)" title="Braid (band)">Braid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas_Is_the_Reason" title="Texas Is the Reason">Texas Is the Reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Arc_(band)" title="Joan of Arc (band)">Joan of Arc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jets_to_Brazil" title="Jets to Brazil">Jets to Brazil</a> and most successfully <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Eat_World" title="Jimmy Eat World">Jimmy Eat World</a>, and by the end of the millennium it was one of the more popular indie styles in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_289-5"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-289">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emo broke into mainstream culture in the early 2000s with the platinum-selling success of Jimmy Eat World's <i><a href="/wiki/Bleed_American" title="Bleed American">Bleed American</a></i> (2001) and <a href="/wiki/Dashboard_Confessional" title="Dashboard Confessional">Dashboard Confessional</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Places_You_Have_Come_to_Fear_the_Most" title="The Places You Have Come to Fear the Most">The Places You Have Come to Fear the Most</a></i> (2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeRogatis2003_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeRogatis2003-291">[291]</a></sup> The new emo had a much more mainstream sound than in the 1990s and a far greater appeal amongst adolescents than its earlier incarnations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeRogatis2003_291-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeRogatis2003-291">[291]</a></sup> At the same time, use of the term emo expanded beyond the musical genre, becoming associated with fashion, a hairstyle and any music that expressed emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> The term emo has been applied by critics and journalists to a variety of artists, including multi-platinum acts such as <a href="/wiki/Fall_Out_Boy" title="Fall Out Boy">Fall Out Boy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chartblog_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-chartblog-293">[293]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/My_Chemical_Romance" title="My Chemical Romance">My Chemical Romance</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> and disparate groups such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramore_(band)" title="Paramore (band)">Paramore</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chartblog_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-chartblog-293">[293]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Panic!_at_the_Disco" title="Panic! at the Disco">Panic! at the Disco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> even when they protest the label. By 2003 post-hardcore bands had also caught the attention of major labels and began to enjoy mainstream success in the album charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMThursday_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMThursday-285">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMThrice_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMThrice-286">[286]</a></sup> A number of these bands were seen as a more aggressive offshoot of emo and given the often vague label of <a href="/wiki/Screamo" title="Screamo">screamo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMScreamo_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMScreamo-296">[296]</a></sup> Around this time, a new wave of post-hardcore bands began to emerge onto the scene that incorporated more pop punk and alternative rock styles into their music, including <a href="/wiki/The_Used" title="The Used">The Used</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hawthorne_Heights" title="Hawthorne Heights">Hawthorne Heights</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Senses_Fail" title="Senses Fail">Senses Fail</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/From_First_to_Last" title="From First to Last">From First to Last</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Emery_(band)" title="Emery (band)">Emery</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup> and Canadian bands <a href="/wiki/Silverstein_(band)" title="Silverstein (band)">Silverstein</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alexisonfire" title="Alexisonfire">Alexisonfire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup> British bands like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funeral_For_A_Friend" title="Funeral For A Friend">Funeral For A Friend</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Blackout_(band)" title="The Blackout (band)">The Blackout</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Enter_Shikari" title="Enter Shikari">Enter Shikari</a> also made headway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Garage_rock.2Fpost-punk_revival">Garage rock/post-punk revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Garage rock/post-punk revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garage_rock_revival" title="Garage rock revival">Garage rock revival</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-punk_revival" title="Post-punk revival">Post-punk revival</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheStrokes.jpg"><img alt="a color photograph of members of the group the Strokes performing on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="1429" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/275px-TheStrokes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/413px-TheStrokes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/550px-TheStrokes.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheStrokes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Strokes" title="The Strokes">The Strokes</a> performing in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 2000s, a new group of bands that played a stripped down and back-to-basics version of guitar rock, emerged into the mainstream. They were variously characterised as part of a garage rock, post-punk or new wave revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> Because the bands came from across the globe, cited diverse influences (from traditional blues, through New Wave to grunge), and adopted differing styles of dress, their unity as a genre has been disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> There had been attempts to revive garage rock and elements of punk in the 1980s and 1990s and by 2000 scenes had grown up in several countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup> The Detroit rock scene included <a href="/wiki/The_Von_Bondies" title="The Von Bondies">the Von Bondies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_Six" title="Electric Six">Electric Six</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Dirtbombs" title="The Dirtbombs">the Dirtbombs</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Detroit_Cobras" title="The Detroit Cobras">the Detroit Cobras</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckley2003VonBondies_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley2003VonBondies-313">[313]</a></sup> and that of New York <a href="/wiki/Radio_4_(band)" title="Radio 4 (band)">Radio 4</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yeah_Yeah_Yeahs" title="Yeah Yeah Yeahs">Yeah Yeah Yeahs</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Rapture_(band)" title="The Rapture (band)">the Rapture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup> Elsewhere, other lesser-known acts such as <a href="/wiki/Billy_Childish" title="Billy Childish">Billy Childish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Buff_Medways" title="The Buff Medways">the Buff Medways</a> from Britain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_(International)_Noise_Conspiracy" title="The (International) Noise Conspiracy">the (International) Noise Conspiracy</a> from Sweden,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_5.6.7.8%27s" title="The 5.6.7.8's">the 5.6.7.8's</a> from Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Oblivians" title="Oblivians">Oblivians</a> from Memphis enjoyed underground, regional or national success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup></p>
<p>The commercial breakthrough from these scenes was led by four bands: <a href="/wiki/The_Strokes" title="The Strokes">the Strokes</a>, who emerged from the New York club scene with their début album <i><a href="/wiki/Is_This_It" title="Is This It">Is This It</a></i> (2001); <a href="/wiki/The_White_Stripes" title="The White Stripes">the White Stripes</a>, from Detroit, with their third album <i><a href="/wiki/White_Blood_Cells_(album)" title="White Blood Cells (album)">White Blood Cells</a></i> (2001); <a href="/wiki/The_Hives" title="The Hives">the Hives</a> from Sweden after their compilation album <i><a href="/wiki/Your_New_Favourite_Band" title="Your New Favourite Band">Your New Favourite Band</a></i> (2001); and <a href="/wiki/The_Vines_(band)" title="The Vines (band)">the Vines</a> from Australia with <i><a href="/wiki/Highly_Evolved" title="Highly Evolved">Highly Evolved</a></i> (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup> They were christened by the media as the "The" bands, and dubbed "The saviours of rock 'n' roll", leading to accusations of hype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> A second wave of bands that gained international recognition due to the movement included <a href="/wiki/Black_Rebel_Motorcycle_Club" title="Black Rebel Motorcycle Club">Black Rebel Motorcycle Club</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Killers" title="The Killers">the Killers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interpol_(band)" title="Interpol (band)">Interpol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Leon" title="Kings of Leon">Kings of Leon</a> from the US,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Libertines" title="The Libertines">the Libertines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Monkeys" title="Arctic Monkeys">Arctic Monkeys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloc_Party" title="Bloc Party">Bloc Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Editors_(band)" title="Editors (band)">Editors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Ferdinand_(band)" title="Franz Ferdinand (band)">Franz Ferdinand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Placebo_(band)" title="Placebo (band)">Placebo</a> from the UK,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jet_(band)" title="Jet (band)">Jet</a> from Australia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Datsuns" title="The Datsuns">the Datsuns</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_D4" title="The D4">the D4</a> from New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_heavy_metal.2C_metalcore_and_retro-metal">Contemporary heavy metal, metalcore and retro-metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Contemporary heavy metal, metalcore and retro-metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal music</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Metalcore" title="Metalcore">Metalcore</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_American_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of American Heavy Metal">New Wave of American Heavy Metal</a></div>
<p>By the new millennium Scandinavia had emerged as one of the areas producing innovative and successful bands, while Belgium, Holland and especially Germany were the most significant markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> Established continental metal bands placed multiple albums in the top 20 of the German charts between 2003 and 2008 included Finnish band <a href="/wiki/Children_of_Bodom" title="Children of Bodom">Children of Bodom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup> Norwegian act <a href="/wiki/Dimmu_Borgir" title="Dimmu Borgir">Dimmu Borgir</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup> Germany's <a href="/wiki/Blind_Guardian" title="Blind Guardian">Blind Guardian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup> and Sweden's <a href="/wiki/HammerFall" title="HammerFall">HammerFall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of two members of the group Children of Bodom standing on a stage with guitars, drums are visible in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/220px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/330px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/440px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Children_of_Bodom" title="Children of Bodom">Children of Bodom</a>, performing at the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Masters_of_Rock_(festival)" title="Masters of Rock (festival)">Masters of Rock</a> festival</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Metalcore, originally an American hybrid of thrash metal and hardcore punk, emerged as a commercial force in the mid-2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup> It was rooted in the <a href="/wiki/Crossover_thrash" title="Crossover thrash">crossover thrash</a> style developed two decades earlier by bands such as <a href="/wiki/Suicidal_Tendencies" title="Suicidal Tendencies">Suicidal Tendencies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dirty_Rotten_Imbeciles" title="Dirty Rotten Imbeciles">Dirty Rotten Imbeciles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stormtroopers_of_Death" title="Stormtroopers of Death">Stormtroopers of Death</a> and remained an underground phenomenon through the 1990s;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> early bands include <a href="/wiki/Earth_Crisis" title="Earth Crisis">Earth Crisis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ig_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-ig-334">[334]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Converge_(band)" title="Converge (band)">Converge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-am-335">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hatebreed" title="Hatebreed">Hatebreed</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rh_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-rh-336">[336]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shai_Hulud_(band)" title="Shai Hulud (band)">Shai Hulud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killyour_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-killyour-337">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Killswitch_Engage" title="Killswitch Engage">Killswitch Engage</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_End_of_Heartache" title="The End of Heartache">The End of Heartache</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Shadows_Fall" title="Shadows Fall">Shadows Fall</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_Within_(Shadows_Fall_album)" title="The War Within (Shadows Fall album)">The War Within</a></i> to debut at number 21 and number 20, respectively, on the <i>Billboard</i> album chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bullet_for_My_Valentine" title="Bullet for My Valentine">Bullet for My Valentine</a>, from Wales, broke into the top 5 in both the U.S. and British charts with <i><a href="/wiki/Scream_Aim_Fire" title="Scream Aim Fire">Scream Aim Fire</a></i> (2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup> Metalcore bands have received prominent slots at <a href="/wiki/Ozzfest" title="Ozzfest">Ozzfest</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Download_Festival" title="Download Festival">Download Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God_(band)" title="Lamb of God (band)">Lamb of God</a>, with a related blend of metal styles, reached number 2 on the <i>Billboard</i> charts in 2009 with <i><a href="/wiki/Wrath_(Lamb_of_God_album)" title="Wrath (Lamb of God album)">Wrath</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup></p>
<p>The success of these bands and others such as <a href="/wiki/Trivium_(band)" title="Trivium (band)">Trivium</a>, who have released both metalcore and straight-ahead thrash albums, and <a href="/wiki/Mastodon_(band)" title="Mastodon (band)">Mastodon</a>, who played in a progressive/<a href="/wiki/Sludge_metal" title="Sludge metal">sludge</a> style, inspired claims of a metal revival in the United States, dubbed by some critics the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_American_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of American Heavy Metal">New Wave of American Heavy Metal</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup> Its roots have been traced to the music of acts like Pantera, Biohazard and Machine Head, drawing on <a href="/wiki/New_York_hardcore" title="New York hardcore">New York hardcore</a>, thrash metal and punk, helping to inspire a move away from the nu metal of the early 2000s and a return to riffs and guitar solos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the early 2010s, metalcore was evolving to more frequently incorporate synthesizers and elements from genres beyond rock and metal. The album <i><a href="/wiki/Reckless_%26_Relentless" title="Reckless &amp; Relentless">Reckless &amp; Relentless</a></i> by British band <a href="/wiki/Asking_Alexandria" title="Asking Alexandria">Asking Alexandria</a> (which sold 31,000 copies in its first week), and The Devil Wears Prada's 2011 album <i><a href="/wiki/Dead_Throne" title="Dead Throne">Dead Throne</a></i> (which sold 32,400 in its first week)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup> reached up to number 9 and 10,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup> respectively, on the <i>Billboard</i> 200 chart. In 2013, British band <a href="/wiki/Bring_Me_the_Horizon" title="Bring Me the Horizon">Bring Me the Horizon</a> released their fourth studio album <a href="/wiki/Sempiternal_(album)" title="Sempiternal (album)">Sempiternal</a> to critical acclaim. The album debuted at number 3 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_Album_Chart" title="UK Album Chart">UK Album Chart</a> and at number 1 in Australia. The album sold 27,522 copies in the US, and charted at number 11 on the US Billboard Chart, making it their highest charting release in America until their follow-up album <a href="/wiki/That%27s_the_Spirit" title="That's the Spirit">That's the Spirit</a> debuted at No. 2 in 2015.</p>
<p>The term "retro-metal" has been applied to such bands as Texas-based <a href="/wiki/The_Sword" title="The Sword">the Sword</a>, California's <a href="/wiki/High_on_Fire" title="High on Fire">High on Fire</a>, Sweden's <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft_(band)" title="Witchcraft (band)">Witchcraft</a>, and Australia's <a href="/wiki/Wolfmother" title="Wolfmother">Wolfmother</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMAgeofWinters_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMAgeofWinters-350">[350]</a></sup> The Sword's <i><a href="/wiki/Age_of_Winters" title="Age of Winters">Age of Winters</a></i> (2006) drew heavily on the work of Black Sabbath and Pentagram,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Begrand2006_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-Begrand2006-351">[351]</a></sup> while Witchcraft added elements of folk rock and psychedelic rock,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup> and Wolfmother's <a href="/wiki/Wolfmother_(album)" title="Wolfmother (album)">self-titled 2005 debut album</a> combined elements of the sounds of Deep Purple and Led Zeppelin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicCosimicEgg_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicCosimicEgg-353">[353]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_electronic_rock">Digital electronic rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Digital electronic rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">Electronic rock</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laptronica" title="Laptronica">Laptronica</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indietronica" title="Indietronica">Indietronica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electroclash" title="Electroclash">Electroclash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dance-punk" title="Dance-punk">Dance-punk</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_rave" title="New rave">New rave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">Synthpop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_(2011).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1728" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg/220px-Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg/330px-Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg/440px-Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_%282011%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ladytron_conference_in_Mexico_(2011).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ladytron" title="Ladytron">Ladytron</a> in 2011. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Reuben_Wu" title="Reuben Wu">Reuben Wu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mira_Aroyo" title="Mira Aroyo">Mira Aroyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Marnie" title="Helen Marnie">Helen Marnie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Hunt" title="Daniel Hunt">Daniel Hunt</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 2000s, as computer technology became more accessible and <a href="/wiki/Music_software" title="Music software">music software</a> advanced, it became possible to create high quality music using little more than a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laptop_computer" title="Laptop computer">laptop computer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._Emmerson.2C_2007_pp._80-1_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-S._Emmerson.2C_2007_pp._80-1-354">[354]</a></sup> This resulted in a massive increase in the amount of home-produced electronic music available to the general public via the expanding internet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup> and new forms of performance such as laptronica<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._Emmerson.2C_2007_pp._80-1_354-1"><a href="#cite_note-S._Emmerson.2C_2007_pp._80-1-354">[354]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Live_coding" title="Live coding">live coding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup> These techniques also began to be used by existing bands, as with <a href="/wiki/Industrial_rock" title="Industrial rock">industrial rock</a> act Nine Inch Nails' album <i><a href="/wiki/Year_Zero_(album)" title="Year Zero (album)">Year Zero</a></i> (2007),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM9InchNails_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM9InchNails-357">[357]</a></sup> and by developing genres that mixed rock with digital techniques and sounds, including indie electronic, electroclash, dance-punk and new rave.</p>
<p>Indie electronic, which had begun in the early 1990s with bands like <a href="/wiki/Stereolab" title="Stereolab">Stereolab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disco_Inferno" title="Disco Inferno">Disco Inferno</a>, took off in the new millennium as the new digital technology developed, with acts including <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_(band)" title="Broadcast (band)">Broadcast</a> from the UK, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justice_(French_band)" title="Justice (French band)">Justice</a> from France, <a href="/wiki/Lali_Puna" title="Lali Puna">Lali Puna</a> from Germany and <a href="/wiki/The_Postal_Service" title="The Postal Service">The Postal Service</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ratatat" title="Ratatat">Ratatat</a> from the US, mixing a variety of indie sounds with electronic music, largely produced on small independent labels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leckart_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leckart-359">[359]</a></sup> The electroclash subgenre began in New York at the end of the 1990s, combining synth pop, techno, punk and performance art. It was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/I-F" title="I-F">I-F</a> with their track "Space Invaders Are Smoking Grass" (1998),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[360]</a></sup> and pursued by artists including <a href="/wiki/Felix_da_Housecat" title="Felix da Housecat">Felix da Housecat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peaches_(musician)" title="Peaches (musician)">Peaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicks_on_Speed" title="Chicks on Speed">Chicks on Speed</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ladytron" title="Ladytron">Ladytron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-post-363">[363]</a></sup> It gained international attention at the beginning of the new millennium and spread to scenes in London and Berlin, but rapidly faded as a recognisable genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup> Dance-punk, mixing post-punk sounds with <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, had developed in the 1980s, but it was revived among some bands of the garage rock/post-punk revival in the early years of the new millennium, particularly among New York acts such as <a href="/wiki/Liars_(band)" title="Liars (band)">Liars</a>, The Rapture and Radio 4, joined by dance-oriented acts who adopted rock sounds such as <a href="/wiki/Out_Hud" title="Out Hud">Out Hud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup> In Britain the combination of indie with dance-punk was dubbed <a href="/wiki/New_rave" title="New rave">new rave</a> in publicity for <a href="/wiki/Klaxons" title="Klaxons">Klaxons</a> and the term was picked up and applied by the <a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a> to bands<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rousing_rave_from_the_grave_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-rousing_rave_from_the_grave-366">[366]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Trash_Fashion" title="Trash Fashion">Trash Fashion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Here_we_glo_again_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-Here_we_glo_again-367">[367]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Young_Pony_Club" title="New Young Pony Club">New Young Pony Club</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_rave.3F_old_rubbish_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_rave.3F_old_rubbish-368">[368]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hadouken!" title="Hadouken!">Hadouken!</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_of_the_Pier" title="Late of the Pier">Late of the Pier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Test_Icicles" title="Test Icicles">Test Icicles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rave_on.2C_just_don.27t_call_it_.27new_rave.27_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rave_on.2C_just_don.27t_call_it_.27new_rave.27-369">[369]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Shitdisco" title="Shitdisco">Shitdisco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rousing_rave_from_the_grave_366-1"><a href="#cite_note-rousing_rave_from_the_grave-366">[366]</a></sup> forming a scene with a similar visual aesthetic to earlier <a href="/wiki/Rave_music" title="Rave music">rave music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rousing_rave_from_the_grave_366-2"><a href="#cite_note-rousing_rave_from_the_grave-366">[366]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_future.27s_bright..._370-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_future.27s_bright...-370">[370]</a></sup></p>
<p>Renewed interest in electronic music and nostalgia for the 1980s led to the beginnings of a synthpop revival, with acts including <a href="/wiki/Adult_(band)" title="Adult (band)">Adult</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fischerspooner" title="Fischerspooner">Fischerspooner</a>. In 2003-4 it began to move into the mainstream with Ladytron, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_Service_(band)" title="Postal Service (band)">Postal Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cut_Copy" title="Cut Copy">Cut Copy</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Bravery" title="The Bravery">the Bravery</a> and, with most commercial success, The Killers all producing records that incorporated vintage synthesizer sounds and styles which contrasted with the dominant sounds of post-grunge and nu-metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cateforis2011pp218-9_371-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cateforis2011pp218-9-371">[371]</a></sup> The style was picked up by a large number of performers, particularly female solo artists, leading the British and other media to proclaim a new era of the female electropop star. Artists named included British acts <a href="/wiki/Little_Boots" title="Little Boots">Little Boots</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Roux" title="La Roux">La Roux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ladyhawke_(musician)" title="Ladyhawke (musician)">Ladyhawke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-372">[372]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewGeneration_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewGeneration-373">[373]</a></sup> Male acts that emerged in the same period included <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Harris" title="Calvin Harris">Calvin Harris</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[374]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frankmusik" title="Frankmusik">Frankmusik</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hurts" title="Hurts">Hurts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[376]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kaskade" title="Kaskade">Kaskade</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LMFAO_(group)" title="LMFAO (group)">LMFAO</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[378]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Owl_City" title="Owl City">Owl City</a>, whose single "<a href="/wiki/Fireflies_(Owl_City_song)" title="Fireflies (Owl City song)">Fireflies</a>" (2009) reached the top of the Billboard chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_impact">Social impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Social impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_effects_of_rock_music" title="Social effects of rock music">Social effects of rock music</a></div>
<p>Different subgenres of rock were adopted by, and became central to, the identity of a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-culture" title="Sub-culture">sub-cultures</a>. In the 1950s and 1960s, respectively, British youths adopted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teddy_Boys" title="Teddy Boys">Teddy Boy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocker_(subculture)" title="Rocker (subculture)">Rocker</a> subcultures, which revolved around US rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake.3B_1990.3B_73-9_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brake.3B_1990.3B_73-9-381">[381]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a> was closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake.3B_1990.3B_73-9_381-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brake.3B_1990.3B_73-9-381">[381]</a></sup> The mid-1970s <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk subculture</a> began in the US, but it was given a distinctive look by British designer <a href="/wiki/Vivienne_Westwood" title="Vivienne Westwood">Vivienne Westwood</a>, a look which spread worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[382]</a></sup> Out of the punk scene, the <a href="/wiki/Goth_subculture" title="Goth subculture">Goth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">Emo</a> subcultures grew, both of which presented distinctive visual styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[383]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG"><img alt="A color photograph showing people from the 1969 Woodstock Festival sitting on grass, in the foreground a back and a white male look at each other" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="700" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/220px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/330px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/440px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1969 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodstock_Festival" title="Woodstock Festival">Woodstock Festival</a> was seen as a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture" title="Counterculture">countercultural</a> lifestyle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When an international rock culture developed, it supplanted <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">cinema</a> as the major sources of fashion influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260-384">[384]</a></sup> Paradoxically, followers of rock music have often mistrusted the world of fashion, which has been seen as elevating image above substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260_384-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260-384">[384]</a></sup> Rock fashions have been seen as combining elements of different cultures and periods, as well as expressing divergent views on sexuality and gender, and rock music in general has been noted and criticised for facilitating greater sexual freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260_384-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al.3B_2000.3B_260-384">[384]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[385]</a></sup> Rock has also been associated with various forms of <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drug</a> use, including the <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamines</a> taken by mods in the early to mid-1960s, through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mescaline" title="Mescaline">mescaline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hashish" title="Hashish">hashish</a> and other hallucinogenic drugs linked with <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a> in the late 1960s and early 1970s; and sometimes to <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a>, all of which have been eulogised in song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[386]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rock has been credited with changing attitudes to race by opening up <a href="/wiki/African-American_culture" title="African-American culture">African-American culture</a> to white audiences; but at the same time, rock has been accused of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">appropriating</a> and exploiting that culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[389]</a></sup> While rock music has absorbed many influences and introduced Western audiences to different musical traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[390]</a></sup> the global spread of rock music has been interpreted as a form of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">cultural imperialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[391]</a></sup> Rock music inherited the folk tradition of <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest song</a>, making political statements on subjects such as war, religion, poverty, civil rights, justice and the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[392]</a></sup> Political activism reached a mainstream peak with the "<a href="/wiki/Do_They_Know_It%27s_Christmas%3F" title="Do They Know It's Christmas?">Do They Know It's Christmas?</a>" single (1984) and <a href="/wiki/Live_Aid" title="Live Aid">Live Aid</a> concert for Ethiopia in 1985, which, while successfully raising awareness of world poverty and funds for aid, have also been criticised (along with similar events), for providing a stage for self-aggrandisement and increased profits for the rock stars involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since its early development rock music has been associated with rebellion against social and political norms, most obviously in early rock and roll's rejection of an adult-dominated culture, the counterculture's rejection of consumerism and conformity and punk's rejection of all forms of social convention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[394]</a></sup> however, it can also be seen as providing a means of commercial exploitation of such ideas and of diverting youth away from political action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Role of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_music#Popular_music" title="Women in music">Women in music § Popular music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="748" data-file-width="528" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/170px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/255px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/340px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro" title="Suzi Quatro">Suzi Quatro</a> is a singer, bassist and bandleader. When she launched her career in 1973, she was one of the few prominent women instrumentalists and bandleaders.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Professional women instrumentalists are uncommon in rock genres such as heavy metal. According to Schaap and Berkers, "playing in a band is largely a male homosocial activity, that is, learning to play in a band is largely a peer-based ... experience, shaped by existing sex-segregated friendship networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[396]</a></sup> They note that rock music "is often defined as a form of male rebellion vis-à-vis female bedroom culture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p102_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p102-397">[397]</a></sup> In popular music, there has been a gendered "distinction between public (male) and private (female) participation" in music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p102_397-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p102-397">[397]</a></sup> "several scholars have argued that men exclude women from bands or from the bands' rehearsals, recordings, performances, and other social activities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-398">[398]</a></sup> "Women are mainly regarded as passive and private consumers of allegedly slick, prefabricated – hence, inferior – pop music..., excluding them from participating as high status rock musicians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_398-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-398">[398]</a></sup> One of the reasons that there are rarely mixed gender bands is that "bands operate as tight-knit units in which homosocial solidarity – social bonds between people of the same sex... – plays a crucial role".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_398-2"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-398">[398]</a></sup> In the 1960s rock music scene, "singing was sometimes an acceptable pastime for a girl, but playing an instrument ... simply wasn't done".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rebeatmag.com_399-0"><a href="#cite_note-rebeatmag.com-399">[399]</a></sup></p>
<p>"The rebellion of rock music was largely a male rebellion; the women—often, in the 1950s and '60s, girls in their teens—in rock usually sang songs as personæ utterly dependent on their macho boyfriends...". Philip Auslander says that "Although there were many women in rock by the late 1960s, most performed only as singers, a traditionally feminine position in popular music". Though some women played instruments in American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-female_bands" title="All-female bands">all-female garage rock bands</a>, none of these bands achieved more than regional success. So they "did not provide viable templates for women's on-going participation in rock".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_400-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-400">[400]</a></sup> In relation to the gender composition of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal bands</a>, it has been said that "[h]eavy metal performers are almost exclusively male"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87.E2.80.9391_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87.E2.80.9391-401">[401]</a></sup> "...at least until the mid-1980s"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[402]</a></sup> apart from "...exceptions such as <a href="/wiki/Girlschool" title="Girlschool">Girlschool</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87.E2.80.9391_401-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87.E2.80.9391-401">[401]</a></sup> However, "...now [in the 2010s] maybe more than ever–strong metal women have put up their dukes and got down to it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[403]</a></sup> "carv[ing] out a considerable place for [them]selves."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[404]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro" title="Suzi Quatro">Suzi Quatro</a> emerged in 1973, "no other prominent female musician worked in rock simultaneously as a singer, instrumentalist, songwriter, and bandleader".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_400-1"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-400">[400]</a></sup> According to Auslander, she was "kicking down the male door in rock and roll and proving that a female <i>musician</i> ... and this is a point I am extremely concerned about ... could play as well if not better than the boys".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_400-2"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-400">[400]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/All-female_band" title="All-female band">all-female band</a> is a musical group in genres such as rock and blues which is exclusively composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_musician" title="Female musician">female musicians</a>. This is distinct from a <a href="/wiki/Girl_group" title="Girl group">girl group</a>, in which the female members are solely vocalists, though this terminology is not universally followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[405]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>
<ul>
<li><img alt="" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="375" height="9" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Open_book_nae_02.svg/16px-Open_book_nae_02.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Open_book_nae_02.svg/24px-Open_book_nae_02.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Open_book_nae_02.svg/32px-Open_book_nae_02.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <i><a href="/wiki/Book:Rock_music" title="Book:Rock music">Rock music</a></i> – <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Books" title="Wikipedia:Books">Wikipedia book</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Turner, Luke. <a class="external text" href="http://thequietus.com/articles/13500-music-golden-age-60s-beatles-dylan-balls" rel="nofollow">How "The Baby Boomers Stole Music With Myths Of A Golden Age"</a>. <i>The Quietus.com</i>. <li id="cite_note-campbell-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-campbell_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-campbell_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Michael Campbell &amp; James Brody, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.co.uk/books?id=RK-JmVbv4OIC&amp;pg=PA80" rel="nofollow">Rock and Roll: An Introduction</a>, pages 80-81</span></li>
